# Prostate Cancer, Nutrition, and Dietary Supplements (PDQ®) - NCI

# Prostate Cancer, Nutrition, and Dietary Supplements (PDQ®)–Health Professional Version

## Introduction

[Go to Patient Version](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/patient/prostate-supplements-pdq)

This [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) information summary provides an overview of the use of various foods and [dietary supplements](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for reducing the risk of developing [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or for treating prostate cancer. This summary includes the history of research, reviews of laboratory and [animal studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454774&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and results of [clinical trials](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45961&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) on the following foods or dietary supplements:

- [Calcium](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_426).
- [Green tea](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_174).
- [Lycopene](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_16).
- [Modified citrus pectin](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_161).
- [Pomegranate](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_162).
- [Selenium](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_283).
- [Soy](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_163).
- [Vitamin D](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_357).
- [Vitamin E](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_328).
- [Multicomponent therapies](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_485).
- [Other prostate health supplements](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_387).

Each type of dietary supplement or food will have a dedicated section in the summary, and new topics will be added over time. Note: A summary on [PC-SPES](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/pc-spes-pdq) is also available.

Prostate cancer is the most common noncutaneous cancer affecting men in the United States. On the basis of data from 2018 to 2021, it is estimated that 12.8% of U.S. men will be diagnosed with prostate cancer during their lifetimes.[[1](#cit/section_1.1)]

Many studies suggest that [complementary and alternative medicine](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44964&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (CAM) use is common among prostate cancer patients, and the use of [vitamins](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44744&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), supplements, and specific foods is frequently reported by these patients. For example, the Prostate CAncer Therapy Selection (PCATS) study was a [prospective](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study that investigated men’s decision-making processes about treatment following a diagnosis of local-[stage](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45885&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer. As part of this study, patients completed surveys regarding CAM use, and more than half of the respondents reported using one or more CAM therapies, with [mind-body modalities](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=729732&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [biologically](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44510&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) based treatments being the most commonly used.[[2](#cit/section_1.2)]

International studies have reported similar findings. A Swedish study published in 2011 found that, overall, participants with prostate cancer were more likely to have used supplements than were healthy population-based [control subjects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44149&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Supplement use was even more common among patients with the healthiest dietary patterns (e.g., high consumption of fatty fish and vegetables).[[3](#cit/section_1.3)] In a Canadian study, CAM use was reported among 39% of recently diagnosed prostate cancer patients, and the most commonly used forms of CAM were [herbals](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463714&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), vitamins, and [minerals](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45787&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Within those categories, [saw palmetto](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=514413&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [vitamin E](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45023&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and [selenium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45113&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were the most popular. The two most popular reasons for choosing CAM were to boost the [immune system](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46356&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and to prevent [recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[4](#cit/section_1.4)] According to another Canadian study, approximately 30% of survey respondents with prostate cancer reported using CAM treatments. In that study, vitamin E, saw palmetto, and [lycopene](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44909&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were most commonly used.[[5](#cit/section_1.5)] A British study published in 2008 indicated that 25% of prostate cancer patients used CAM, with the most frequently reported interventions being low-fat [diets](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), vitamins, and lycopene. The majority of CAM users in this study cited improving [quality of life](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45417&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and boosting the immune system as the main reasons they used CAM.[[6](#cit/section_1.6)]

Vitamin and supplement use has also been documented in men at risk of developing prostate cancer. One study examined vitamin and supplement use in men with a [family history](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=642015&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) of prostate cancer. At the time of the survey, almost 60% of the men were using vitamins or supplements. One-third of the men were using vitamins and supplements that were specifically marketed for prostate health or [chemoprevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (e.g., selenium, green tea, and saw palmetto).[[7](#cit/section_1.7)] A 2004 study examined herbal and vitamin supplement use in men who attended a prostate cancer [screening](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46171&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) clinic. Men who attended the screening clinic completed questionnaires about supplement use. Of the respondents, [analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390238&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) revealed that a reported 70% used multivitamins, and 21% used herbal supplements.[[8](#cit/section_1.8)]

A [meta-analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) published in 2008 reviewed studies that reported vitamin and mineral supplement use among cancer [survivors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=450125&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The results showed that, among prostate cancer survivors, vitamin or mineral use ranged from 26% to 35%.[[9](#cit/section_1.9)]

Although many prostate cancer patients use CAM treatments, they do not all disclose their CAM use to treating [physicians.](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390246&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) According to results from the PCATS study, 43% of patients discussed their CAM use with a health care professional.[[2](#cit/section_1.2)] In two separate studies, 58% of respondents told their doctors about their CAM usage.[[4](#cit/section_1.4),[6](#cit/section_1.6)]

How do prostate cancer patients decide whether or not to use CAM? A qualitative study published in 2005 described results from interviews with prostate cancer patients. The study identified differences in thinking patterns between CAM users and nonusers and suggested that no specific theme led patients to CAM; instead, patients were directed by a combination of ideas. For example, the perception of CAM as harmless was associated with the belief that [conventional medicine](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=449752&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) resulted in many negative [side effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46580&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[10](#cit/section_1.10)] Results of a 2003 qualitative study suggested that decision making about CAM treatments by prostate cancer patients depended on both fixed (e.g., [medical history](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=689078&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) and flexible (e.g., a need to feel in control) decision factors.[[11](#cit/section_1.11)]

Many of the medical and scientific terms used in this summary are hypertext linked (at first use in each section) to the [NCI Dictionary of Cancer Terms](https://www.cancer.gov/publications/dictionaries/cancer-terms/ "https://www.cancer.gov/publications/dictionaries/cancer-terms/"), which is oriented toward nonexperts. When a linked term is clicked, a definition will appear in a separate window.

Reference citations in some PDQ cancer information summaries may include links to external websites that are operated by individuals or organizations for the purpose of marketing or advocating the use of specific treatments or products. These reference citations are included for informational purposes only. Their inclusion should not be considered an endorsement of the content of the websites, or of any treatment or product, by the PDQ Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies Editorial Board or the [National Cancer Institute](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44266&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

For more information, see [Prostate Cancer Prevention](/types/prostate/hp/prostate-prevention-pdq).

## Calcium

### Overview

This section contains the following key information:

- [Calcium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45632&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is required for certain [metabolic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44056&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) functions such as vascular contraction and vasodilation, muscle function, [nerve](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373935&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) transmission, [intracellular](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44047&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) signaling, and hormonal secretion.
- Major sources of calcium in the United States are food and [dietary supplements](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Studies of the association between calcium and [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have been limited to [nutritional](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44698&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) sources of calcium, such as dairy products.
- Some studies suggest that high total calcium intake may be associated with increased risk of advanced and [metastatic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44058&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer, compared with lower intake of calcium.
- Additional research is needed to clarify the effects of calcium and/or dairy products on prostate cancer risk.

### General Information and History

Calcium, the most abundant [mineral](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45787&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in the body, is found in some foods, added to others, available as a dietary supplement, and present in some [medicines](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=482419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (such as antacids). Calcium is required for vascular contraction and vasodilation, muscle function, nerve transmission, intracellular signaling, and hormonal secretion, although less than 1% of total body calcium is needed to support these critical metabolic functions.[[1](#cit/section_2.1)] [Serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) calcium is very tightly regulated and does not fluctuate with changes in dietary intake; the body uses bone [tissue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46683&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) as a reservoir for, and source of, calcium to maintain constant concentrations of calcium in [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), muscle, and intercellular [fluids](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44669&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[1](#cit/section_2.1)]

The major sources of calcium in the U.S. population are food and dietary supplements.[[2](#cit/section_2.2)] According to recent National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey data, U.S. adults obtain 38% of their dietary calcium from milk and milk products, such as yogurt and cheese.[[3](#cit/section_2.3)] Nondairy sources include vegetables, such as Chinese cabbage, kale, and broccoli. Spinach provides calcium, but its [bioavailability](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is poor. Most grains do not have high amounts of calcium unless they are fortified; however, they contribute calcium to the [diet](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) because they contain small amounts of calcium, and people consume them frequently. Foods fortified with calcium include many fruit juices and drinks, tofu, and cereals. In the United States, dietary supplements, including calcium supplements, are commonly used to prevent [chronic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45641&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) diseases, including [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[1](#cit/section_2.1)] [Mean](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285973&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary calcium intakes for males aged 1 year and older ranged from 871 to 1,266 mg/day depending on life stage group (i.e., infant, [adolescent](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=776825&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), or adult). About 43% of the U.S. population uses dietary supplements containing calcium, which increases calcium intake by about 330 mg/day among supplement users.[[1](#cit/section_2.1),[2](#cit/section_2.2)]

To evaluate the association between calcium intake and prostate cancer [mortality](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=496502&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [morbidity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44514&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), it may be important to assess objective, [biological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44510&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [markers](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45776&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of calcium, include data that account for nutritional and supplemental calcium intake, and control for other confounding factors. However, studies of the association between calcium and prostate cancer have been limited to nutritional sources of calcium, such as dairy products. Although more than half of the U.S. population uses [vitamin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44744&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and mineral supplements (at an annual cost of over $11 billion), few studies include supplement use in the association of disease risk, including prostate cancer or mortality rates.[[1](#cit/section_2.1),[2](#cit/section_2.2)] For more information, see [Prostate Cancer Prevention](/types/prostate/hp/prostate-prevention-pdq).

Companies distribute calcium as a dietary supplement. In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. The FDA can remove dietary supplements from the market that are deemed unsafe. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of calcium as a treatment for cancer.

### Preclinical/Animal Studies

#### *In vitro* studies

[Prostate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46539&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [cancer cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=695994&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were treated with bovine milk, almond milk, [soy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407766&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) milk, casein, or [lactose](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44686&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in a 2011 study. Treatment with bovine milk resulted in growth stimulation of LNCaP prostate cancer cells. Growth of prostate cancer cells was not affected by treatment with soy milk, and treatment with almond milk resulted in growth inhibition.[[4](#cit/section_2.4)]

#### *In vivo* studies

One study investigated the effects of dietary calcium on prostate [tumor progression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45669&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in LPB-Tag [transgenic mice](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691466&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The animals consumed low (0.2%) or high (2.0%) calcium diets and were sacrificed at age 5, 7, or 9 weeks. [Tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) weight and progression were similar in mice that were fed low- and high-calcium diets.[[5](#cit/section_2.5)]

A 2012 study examined the impact of dietary [vitamin D](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=427253&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and calcium on prostate cancer growth in [athymic mice](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44579&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The mice were [injected](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44678&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with human prostate cancer cells and were randomly assigned to receive specific diets (e.g., high calcium/vitamin D or normal calcium/no vitamin D). The mice that received the normal calcium/vitamin D-deficient diet exhibited significantly greater (*P* < .05) tumor volumes than did mice that received the other diets.[[6](#cit/section_2.6)]

### Human Studies

#### Epidemiological studies

Several [epidemiological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies have found an association between high intakes of calcium, dairy foods, or both, and an increased risk of developing prostate cancer.[[7](#cit/section_2.7)-[9](#cit/section_2.9)] However, others have found only a weak relationship, no relationship, or a negative association between calcium intake and prostate cancer risk.[[10](#cit/section_2.10)-[14](#cit/section_2.14)] A 2022 [prospective cohort study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=286693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) examined 28,737 men who belong to the Seventh-day Adventist church. These men had wide ranges of dairy and calcium intake. The study found that a higher intake of dairy foods or other potentially causal factors associated with dairy intake were associated with a higher risk of prostate cancer. This was not true for nondairy sources of calcium. On the basis of these studies, interpretation of the evidence is complicated by the difficulty of separating the effects of dairy products from the effects of calcium. Additionally, earlier epidemiological studies had several limitations. The association between dairy foods, calcium intake, and prostate cancer was limited to evidence from self-reported food frequency questionnaires of nutritional sources of calcium, with a focus on dairy foods.[[14](#cit/section_2.14)-[16](#cit/section_2.16)] Competing [risk factors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45873&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), such as other major [nutrients](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44697&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in dairy (i.e., total fats, saturated fats, [calories](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44651&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) and [concomitant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=635464&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and confounding factors (i.e., age, [body mass index](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=455136&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [steroid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=613764&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [hormones](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45713&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and other metabolic events in the causal pathway) were not accounted for. Additionally, no objective markers of calcium, such as serum calcium, were obtained from these [cohorts](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=561393&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). [Observational studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=286105&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) overall, however, suggest that high total calcium intake may be associated with increased risk of advanced and metastatic prostate cancer, compared with lower intake of calcium.[[11](#cit/section_2.11),[12](#cit/section_2.12),[17](#cit/section_2.17)-[19](#cit/section_2.19)] Another [analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390238&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of 886 [prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45851&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) patients found an increased risk of being [diagnosed](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46450&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with more [aggressive](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46053&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) disease in men with higher calcium intakes.[[20](#cit/section_2.20)] The hazard of disease [recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) after [surgical](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45570&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) treatment was increased in men with both very low and high calcium intakes. Additional research is needed to clarify the effects of calcium and/or dairy products on prostate cancer risk and to elucidate potential biological mechanisms.

#### Interventional studies

In a [randomized clinical trial](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) published in 2005, 672 men received either 3 g of [calcium carbonate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45239&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (1,200 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) calcium) or [placebo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46688&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) daily for 4 years and were followed for 12 years. During the first 6 years of the study, there were significantly fewer prostate cancer cases in the calcium group compared with the placebo group. However, this difference was no longer [statistically significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44167&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) at the 10-year evaluation.[[21](#cit/section_2.21)]

#### Meta-analyses

A [meta-analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) published in 2005 reported that there may be an association between increased risk of prostate cancer and greater consumption of dairy products and calcium.[[22](#cit/section_2.22)]

A 2008 meta-analysis reviewed 45 observational studies and found no evidence of a link between dairy products and risk of prostate cancer.[[23](#cit/section_2.23)] A meta-analysis of [cohort studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) published between 1996 and 2006 found a positive association between milk and dairy product consumption and risk of prostate cancer.[[24](#cit/section_2.24)]

In a recent review, the U.S. Preventive Services Task Force Evidence Syntheses, formerly Systematic Evidence Reviews, conducted meta-analyses using Mantel-Haenszel fixed effects models for overall cancer [incidence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46145&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [cardiovascular disease](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=748137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) incidence, and all-cause mortality. Vitamin D and/or calcium [supplementation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45916&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) showed no overall effect on cancer incidence and mortality, including prostate cancer.[[3](#cit/section_2.3)] In a meta-analysis of the association of calcium without the coadministration of vitamin D, a reduced risk of prostate cancer was observed, although there were only a few events.[[25](#cit/section_2.25)]

In 2007, the World Cancer Research Fund/American Institute for Cancer Research reported that there was probable evidence that diets high in calcium increase the risk of prostate cancer and that there is limited suggestive evidence that milk and dairy products also increase the risk.[[26](#cit/section_2.26)] Since publication, 18 additional studies that evaluated dairy or calcium intake and the risk of prostate cancer have been published. A 2015 meta-analysis of this literature concluded that high intakes of dairy products, milk, low-fat milk, cheese, total dietary calcium, and dairy calcium may increase prostate cancer risk.[[27](#cit/section_2.27)] Supplemental calcium and nondairy calcium were not associated with an increased risk, although supplemental calcium was associated with an increased risk of fatal prostate cancer. The authors suggested that this association needs additional study.

#### Current Clinical Trials

Use our [advanced clinical trial search](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced") to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. [General information](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials") about clinical trials is also available.

## Green Tea

### Overview

This section contains the following key information:

- Green tea is produced by a process of steaming and drying the leaves from the *Camellia sinensis* (L.) plant.
- Some [research](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651211&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) results suggest that green tea may have a protective effect against [cardiovascular](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44005&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) diseases and against various forms of [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), including [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- [Catechins](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686469&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) are phenolic [compounds](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=422394&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in tea that have been associated with many of green tea’s proposed health benefits.
- Green tea catechins (GTCs) include (−)-epigallocatechin-3-gallate ([EGCG](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=687277&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)), (−)-epicatechin (EC), (−)-epigallocatechin (EGC), (−)-epicatechin-3-gallate (ECG), but also include oligomeric proanthocyanidins derived from these catechin [monomers](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=601171&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- [Laboratory](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44512&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [preclinical](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and early-phase [clinical trials](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45961&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have identified EGCG as one of the most potent modulators of [molecular pathways](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=561605&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) thought to be relevant to [prostate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46539&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [carcinogenesis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). EGCG has been shown to act as an [androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [antagonist](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=350250&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and can suppress prostate cancer [cell proliferation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46479&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), suppress production of [prostate-specific antigen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46540&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (PSA) by prostate cancer [cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and demonstrate potent and selective proapoptotic activity in prostate cancer [cell lines](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=695994&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) *[in vitro](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).*
- [Oral](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44068&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) intake of either a GTC solution or EGCG alone was associated with significant reductions in [tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) size, reduced [multiplicity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=372933&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and reduced development of prostate cancer in studies with [transgenic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691466&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [adenocarcinoma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46216&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the mouse prostate (TRAMP) mice.
- In Asian countries with a high *per capita* consumption of green tea, prostate cancer [mortality](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=496502&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) rates are among the lowest in the world, and the risk of prostate cancer appears to be increased among Asian men who abandon their original dietary habits upon migrating to the United States. [Case-control](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=348989&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [cohort studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), so far, have variously shown beneficial or neutral results, with the exception of one study that showed an increased risk of developing advanced prostate cancer with consumption of green tea.
- GTCs have been well tolerated in [clinical studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44195&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that target healthy male subjects, men with precursor [lesions](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46324&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and men with prostate cancer. [Side effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46580&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were reduced with a decaffeinated formulation and when green tea was consumed in nonfasting conditions. The most common side effects related to GTC were mild [gastrointestinal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45692&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [symptoms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45022&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- At least two [randomized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [controlled](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44014&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) trials have shown an overall decreased rate of [progression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44078&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) to atypical small acinar proliferation or prostate cancer in men with [high-grade](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=386205&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44345&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (HGPIN) treated with GTCs.

### General Information and History

Sailors first brought tea to England in 1644, although tea has been popular in Asia since ancient times. After water, tea is the most-consumed beverage in the world.[[1](#cit/section_3.1)] Tea originates from the *C. sinensis* plant, and the process methods of the leaves determine the type of tea produced. Green tea is not fermented but is made by an [enzyme](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46081&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) deactivation step whereby intensive heat (i.e., roasting the freshly collected tea leaves in a wok or, historically, steaming the leaves) is applied to preserve the tea's [polyphenols](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=256573&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (catechins) and freshness. In contrast, the enzyme-catalyzed polymerization and [oxidation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44491&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of catechins and other components produce darker-colored black tea.[[2](#cit/section_3.2)] Oolong, a third major type of tea, which is dark/black rather than green as a result of being partially fermented, contains partially [oxidized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=654996&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) catechins.[[1](#cit/section_3.1)]

In this summary, *tea* refers to the leaves of the *C. sinensis* plant or the beverage brewed from those leaves.

Some observational and interventional studies suggest that green tea may have a protective effect against [cardiovascular disease](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=748137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov),[[3](#cit/section_3.3)] and there is evidence that green tea may protect against various forms of cancer.[[4](#cit/section_3.4)] Many of the health benefits associated with tea have been attributed to polyphenols. GTCs include EGCG, EC, EGC, ECG, and oligomeric proanthocyanidins derived from these catechin monomers. Among these compounds, EGCG is the most abundant catechin in green tea and has been widely researched;[[5](#cit/section_3.5)] however, it is also classified as a promiscuous compound.[[6](#cit/section_3.6)] Laboratory, preclinical, and early-phase clinical trials have identified EGCG as one of the most potent modulators of molecular pathways thought to be relevant to prostate carcinogenesis.[[5](#cit/section_3.5)] Tea leaves also contain considerable amounts of oligomeric catechins, in particular, oligomeric proanthocyanidins. Together with the catechin monomers, they constitute the green tea polyphenols (GTPs). GTP composition and the ratio of monomeric to oligomeric catechins can vary widely, depending on processing and source of the tea leaves. Considering that EGCG and other monomeric catechins interfere with *in vitro* assays and exhibit a wide range of [biological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44510&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects,[[6](#cit/section_3.6),[7](#cit/section_3.7)] this indicates that the chemical factors responsible for the actual *[in vivo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46352&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* health benefits of green tea are mostly unknown.

Several companies distribute green tea as a [dietary supplement](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. The FDA can remove dietary supplements from the market that are deemed unsafe. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of green tea as a treatment for cancer or any other medical [condition](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651193&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

### Preclinical/Animal Studies

#### *In vitro* studies

Prostate cancer cells treated with EGCG (concentrations, 0–80 [μM](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=659790&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) demonstrated suppressed cell proliferation and decreased levels of PSA [protein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46092&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [mRNA](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=662001&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in the presence or absence of androgen.[[8](#cit/section_3.8)]

In a 2011 study, human prostate cancer cells were treated initially with EGCG (concentrations, 1.5–7.5 μM) and then with [radiation.](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45072&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) The results showed that exposing cells to EGCG for 30 minutes before radiation significantly reduced [apoptosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46524&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), compared with radiation alone.[[9](#cit/section_3.9)]

In another study, prostate cancer cells treated with EGCG (0–50 μM) exhibited [dose-dependent](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44110&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) decreases in cellular proliferation and increases in extracellular signal-regulated [kinase](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=641114&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (ERK) 1/2 activity. To further examine the effect of EGCG on the ERK 1/2 pathway, cells were treated with EGCG (0–50 μM) and a mitogen-activated protein kinase ([MEK](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=600473&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) inhibitor or phosphoinositide-3 kinase ([PI3K](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=530323&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) inhibitor. Inhibition of MEK did not prevent ERK 1/2 upregulation, although the increase in ERK 1/2 after EGCG treatment was partially inhibited with the PI3K inhibitor. These findings suggest that EGCG may prevent prostate cancer cell proliferation by increasing the activity of ERK 1/2 via a MEK-independent, PI3K-dependent mechanism.[[10](#cit/section_3.10)]

According to a 2010 study, EGCG treatment (20–120 μM) resulted in changes in expression levels of 40 [genes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in prostate cancer cells, including a fourfold downregulation of inhibitor of [DNA](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45671&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) binding 2 (ID2; a protein involved in cell proliferation and survival). In addition, forced expression of ID2 in cells treated with 80 μM EGCG resulted in reduced apoptosis, suggesting that EGCG may cause cell death via an ID2-related mechanism.[[11](#cit/section_3.11)]

Advances in [nanotechnology](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445071&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)—*nanochemoprevention*—may result in more-effective [administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=478733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of EGCG to men at risk of developing prostate cancer. Prostate cancer cells were treated with EGCG-loaded (100 μM EGCG) [nanoparticles](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653131&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or free EGCG. Although both treatments decreased cell proliferation and induced apoptosis, the nanoparticle treatment had a greater effect at a lower [concentration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=683342&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) than did free EGCG. This finding suggests that using a nanoparticle delivery system for EGCG may increase its [bioavailability](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and improve its [chemopreventive](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) actions.[[12](#cit/section_3.12)] In one study, EGCG (30 μM) was [encapsulated](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46437&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in nanoparticles that contained polymers targeting prostate-specific [membrane](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46294&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [antigen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46086&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (PSMA). Prostate cancer cells treated with this [intervention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454757&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) exhibited decreases in proliferation; however, the intervention did not affect [nonmalignant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44217&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) control cells. The results suggest that this delivery system may be effective for selective targeting of prostate cancer cells.[[13](#cit/section_3.13)]

Research also suggests that [glutathione-S-transferase](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373012&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) pi (GSTP1) may be a [tumor suppressor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46657&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) and that hypermethylation of certain regions of this gene (i.e., CpG islands) may be a [molecular marker](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=579630&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer. Increased [methylation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655031&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) leads to silencing of the gene. A set of experiments investigated the effects of green tea polyphenols on GSTP1 expression. Treatment of different types of prostate cancer cells with green tea polyphenols (1–10 [μg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=306521&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/[mL](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655103&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [Polyphenon E](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=350240&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) resulted in re-expression of GSTP1 by reversing hypermethylation and by reducing expression of methyl-CpG–binding [domain](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460145&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) proteins, which bind to methylated DNA. These results indicate that green tea polyphenols may have chemopreventive effects via actions on gene-silencing processes.[[14](#cit/section_3.14)]

The results of a 2011 study suggested that green tea polyphenols may exert anticancer effects by inhibiting [histone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=318814&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) deacetylases ([HDACs](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=586370&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)). Class I HDACs are often overexpressed in various cancers, including prostate cancer. Treatment of human prostate cancer cells with green tea polyphenols (10–80 μg/mL Polyphenon E) resulted in decreased class I HDAC activity and increased expression of Bax, a proapoptotic protein.[[15](#cit/section_3.15)]

Owing to the high concentrations of tea polyphenols used in some of the *in vitro* experiments, results should be interpreted with caution. Studies in humans have indicated that [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels of EGCG are 0.1 to 0.6 [µM](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=659790&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) after consumption of two to three cups of green tea and that drinking seven to nine cups of green tea results in EGCG blood levels still lower than 1 μM.[[16](#cit/section_3.16),[17](#cit/section_3.17)]

#### Animal studies

[Animal models](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43996&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have been used in several studies investigating the effects of green tea on prostate cancer. In one study, TRAMP mice were given access to water or GTC–treated water (0.3% GTC solution; this exposure mimics human consumption of 6 cups of green tea daily). After 24 weeks, water-fed TRAMP mice had developed prostate cancer, whereas mice treated with GTCs showed only prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia lesions, suggesting that GTCs may help delay the development of prostate tumors.[[18](#cit/section_3.18)] In another study, castrated mice were [injected](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44678&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with prostate cancer cells and then treated daily with [intraperitoneal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46339&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [injections](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44678&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of 1 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) EGCG or vehicle. Treatment with EGCG resulted in reductions in [tumor volume](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467873&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and decreases in [serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) PSA levels compared with vehicle treatment.

In a 2011 study, EGCG was shown to be an [androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) antagonist; when added to prostate cancer cells, EGCG physically interacted with the androgen receptor’s ligand-binding domain. In addition, mice [implanted](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285636&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with tumor cells and treated with EGCG (intraperitoneal injections of 1 mg EGCG, 3/wk) exhibited less [androgen receptor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=757143&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [protein expression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=557359&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) than did mice that were treated with vehicle.[[19](#cit/section_3.19)]

In a 2009 study, TRAMP mice were started on a green tea polyphenol intervention (0.1% green tea polyphenols in drinking water) at various ages (meant to represent different [stages](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45885&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer development).[[20](#cit/section_3.20)] The results showed that, although all of the green tea–fed mice exhibited longer tumor-free survival than did water-fed control mice, there was an advantage for the mice that were fed with green tea the longest.[[20](#cit/section_3.20)] In one study, EGCG treatment (0.06% EGCG in drinking water; this exposure mimics human consumption of 6 cups of green tea/d) was initiated in TRAMP mice at age 12 or 28 weeks. EGCG treatment suppressed HGPIN in mice treated at age 12 weeks; however, EGCG did not prevent prostate cancer development in mice that began treatment at age 28 weeks.[[21](#cit/section_3.21)]

Using the TRAMP mouse model,[[22](#cit/section_3.22)] one study demonstrated that oral infusion of GTP extract at a human-achievable dose (equivalent to 6 cups of green tea/d) significantly delayed [primary tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45847&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) incidence and [tumor burden](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44627&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), as assessed sequentially by [magnetic resonance imaging](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45997&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov); decreased prostate weight (64% of [baseline](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) and genitourinary weight (72%); inhibited serum [insulin-like growth factor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653119&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (IGF)-1; restored insulin-like growth factor–binding protein-3 (IGFBP-3) levels; and produced marked reduction in the protein expression of proliferating cell nuclear antigen in the GTP-fed TRAMP mice, compared with water-fed TRAMP mice. Furthermore, GTP consumption caused significant apoptosis, which possibly resulted in reduced dissemination of cancer cells, thereby causing inhibition of development, progression, and [metastasis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46710&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) to distant [organ](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257523&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) sites. In another study, 119 male TRAMP mice and 119 C57BL/6J mice were treated orally with one of three doses of Polyphenon E (200, 500, or 1,000 mg/kg/d) in drinking water *ad libitum*, replicating human-achievable doses. Safety and [efficacy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=346517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) assessments were performed at baseline and when mice were 12, 22, and 32 weeks old. Results indicated that the number and size of tumors in treated TRAMP mice were significantly decreased, compared with untreated animals. In untreated 32-week-old TRAMP mice, prostate carcinoma metastasis to distant sites was observed in 100% of mice (8/8), compared with 13% of mice (2/16) treated with high-dose Polyphenon E during the same period.[[23](#cit/section_3.23)]

#### Animal safety studies

In a [National Cancer Institute](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44266&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (NCI) Division of Cancer Prevention (DCP)–sponsored, 9-month, oral [toxicity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445093&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study, Polyphenon E was administered (200, 500, or 1,000 mg/kg/d) to fasted male and female beagle dogs. The study was terminated prematurely because of excessive loss of animals due to [morbidity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44514&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and mortality in all treatment groups. These studies have revealed some unique [dose-limiting](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44170&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) lethal [liver](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46312&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), gastrointestinal, and renal toxicities. Gross necropsy revealed therapy-induced lesions in the gastrointestinal tracts, livers, kidneys, reproductive organs, and [hematopoietic tissues](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44036&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of treated male and female dogs. In the 13-week [follow-up](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44671&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study, the no-observed-adverse-effect–level was greater than 600 mg/kg per day of Polyphenon E.[[24](#cit/section_3.24)] When the study was conducted in nonfasted dogs under the same testing conditions and dose levels, the results were unremarkable. Nonspecific toxicity and a tenfold reduction in the [maximum tolerated dose](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=546597&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in fasted beagle dogs compared with fed beagle dogs were seen using a purified GTC containing less than 77% EGCG.[[25](#cit/section_3.25)] However, in the follow-up NCI DCP–sponsored study, which compared fed dogs with fasted dogs using several Polyphenon E formulations, no deaths occurred, suggesting that fasting may have rendered the target organ systems more vulnerable to the effects of [green tea extract](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=389271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

In a study [[23](#cit/section_3.23)] of several doses of a standardized Polyphenon E targeting TRAMP mice, no liver or other toxicities were observed. Long-term (32 weeks) treatment with Polyphenon E (200, 500, and 1,000 mg/kg/d) was safe and well tolerated, with no evidence of toxicity in C57BL/6J mice. The C57BL/6J mice showed no differences in appearance or behavior, or changes in prostate and body weights after 32 weeks of treatment for all three doses of Polyphenon E. No discernible [histopathological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467841&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) changes were observed in the liver, [lung](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270740&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), or any prostate [lobe](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46188&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of C57BL/6J mice treated with the three different doses of Polyphenon E.[[23](#cit/section_3.23)] Similarly, another [preclinical study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [[26](#cit/section_3.26)] did not observe liver or other toxicities with standardized EGCG at doses of up to 500 mg EGCG preparation/kg per day.

### Human Studies

#### Epidemiological studies

The relationship between green tea intake and prostate cancer has been examined in several [epidemiological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies.

Two [meta-analyses](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) examined the consumption of green tea and prostate cancer risk, with one meta-analysis including black tea.[[27](#cit/section_3.27),[28](#cit/section_3.28)] For green tea, seven [observational studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=286105&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were identified, and most were from Asia. The results indicated a [statistically significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44167&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) inverse association between green tea consumption and prostate cancer risk in the three case-control studies, but no association was found in the four cohort studies. For black tea, no association was found between black tea consumption and prostate cancer risk.[[27](#cit/section_3.27)] The inconsistent results reported in these [population studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=561718&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may be attributed to confounding factors that include the following:[[29](#cit/section_3.29)-[33](#cit/section_3.33)]

- Consumption of salted or very hot tea.
- Geographical location.
- [Tobacco](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439432&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) use.
- [Alcohol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463134&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) use.
- Other dietary differences.

In Asian countries with a high *per capita* consumption of green tea, prostate cancer mortality rates are among the lowest in the world,[[34](#cit/section_3.34)] and the risk of prostate cancer appears to be increased among Asian men who abandon their original dietary habits upon migrating to the United States.[[34](#cit/section_3.34)] Overall, findings from population studies suggest that green tea may help protect against prostate cancer in Asian populations.[[27](#cit/section_3.27),[35](#cit/section_3.35)] Currently, there are no epidemiological studies in other populations examining the association between green tea consumption and prostate cancer risk or protection from risk. With the increasing consumption of green tea worldwide, including by the U.S. population, emerging data from ongoing studies will further contribute to defining the cancer preventive activity of green tea or GTCs.

#### Interventional studies

##### Bioavailability

[Phase I/II](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45832&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) intervention studies have reported bioavailability of EGCG in [plasma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45839&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) using single and repeated doses of EGCG, noting higher plasma EGCG concentrations in fasting conditions relative to fed conditions.[[36](#cit/section_3.36)-[38](#cit/section_3.38)] Studies using varying doses (400 mg, 800 mg EGCG) of GTCs and Polyphenon E administered in single and repeated dosing schedules for 3 to 6 weeks have reported [median](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44941&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) maximum concentrations of EGCG ranging from 68.8 [ng](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285977&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/mL to 390.36 ng/mL (see [Table 1](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_609)).[[38](#cit/section_3.38)-[40](#cit/section_3.40)] Not all individuals in the treatment arms of these and other studies [[31](#cit/section_3.31),[41](#cit/section_3.41),[42](#cit/section_3.42)] had detectable levels of EGCG, indicating potential variation in individual absorption. Catechins other than EGCG were nondetectable or below quantifiable levels in the plasma in many trials.

Catechin tissue levels have also been reported, and high variations were quite common. Notably, catechin levels in prostate tissue were low to undetectable after the administration of Polyphenon E in one preprostatectomy study.[[39](#cit/section_3.39)] An [analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390238&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate tissue obtained from the green tea drinkers revealed that both [methylated](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655031&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and nonmethylated forms of EGCG are found in the prostate following a short-term treatment with green tea, with 48% of EGCG in the methylated form.[[39](#cit/section_3.39)] Methylated forms of EGCG are not as effective as EGCG in inhibiting cell proliferation and inducing apoptosis in prostate cancer cells, suggesting that methylation status of EGCG may affect the chemopreventive properties of green tea. Methylation status may be determined by [polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44805&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) of the [catechol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44098&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-O-methyltransferase (COMT; the enzyme that methylates EGCG) gene.[[43](#cit/section_3.43)]

| Source | EGCG Dose | Condition | Duration | Median Plasma EGCG Concentration (ng/mL) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  |  |  |  |  |
