## Selenium

### Overview

This section contains the following key information:

- [Selenium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45113&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is an essential trace [mineral](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45787&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) involved in a number of [biological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44510&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) processes, including [enzyme](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46081&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) regulation, [gene expression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=537335&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and [immune function](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45721&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- [Animal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454774&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [epidemiological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies have suggested there may be an inverse relationship between selenium [supplementation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45916&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) risk.
- The results of epidemiological studies suggest some complexity in the association between [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels of selenium and the risk of developing [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- The Selenium and Vitamin E Cancer Prevention Trial (SELECT), a large [multicenter](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44939&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [clinical trial](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45961&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), was initiated to examine the effects of selenium and/or [vitamin E](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45023&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) on the development of prostate cancer.
- Initial results of SELECT, published in 2009, showed no [statistically significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44167&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) difference in the rate of prostate cancer in men who were randomly assigned to receive the selenium supplements.
- In 2011, updated results from SELECT showed no [significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects of selenium supplementation on prostate cancer risk, but men who took vitamin E alone had a 17% increase in prostate cancer risk compared with men who took a placebo.
- In 2014, an analysis of SELECT results showed that men who had high selenium status at [baseline](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and who were randomly assigned to receive selenium supplementation had an increased risk of [high-grade](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=386205&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer.

### General Information and History

Selenium is an essential trace mineral involved in a number of biological processes, including enzyme regulation, gene expression, and immune function. Selenium was discovered in 1818 and named after the Greek goddess of the moon, Selene.[[1](#cit/section_7.1)] A number of selenoproteins have been identified in humans, including selenoprotein P (SEPP), which is the main selenium carrier in the body and is important for selenium [homeostasis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=449741&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

Food sources of selenium include meat, vegetables, and nuts. The selenium content of the soil where food is raised determines the amount of selenium found in plants and animals. For adults, the recommended daily allowance for selenium is 55 [µg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=306521&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[2](#cit/section_7.2)] Most [dietary](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) selenium occurs as selenocysteine or selenomethionine.[[1](#cit/section_7.1)] Selenium accumulates in the [thyroid gland](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44751&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [liver](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46312&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [pancreas](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46254&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [pituitary gland](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46229&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and renal medulla.[[3](#cit/section_7.3)]

Selenium is a component of the enzyme [glutathione](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373010&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) peroxidase, an enzyme that functions as an [antioxidant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43997&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[4](#cit/section_7.4)] However, at high [concentrations](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=683342&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), selenium may function as a [pro-oxidant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=372940&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[2](#cit/section_7.2)]

Selenium is implicated in a number of disease states. Selenium [deficiency](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=321364&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may result in Keshan disease, a form of childhood cardiomyopathy, and Kaskin-Beck disease, a bone [disorder](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407758&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[5](#cit/section_7.5)] Some clinical trials have suggested that high levels of selenium may be associated with [diabetes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44911&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [[6](#cit/section_7.6)] and high [cholesterol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407756&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[2](#cit/section_7.2)]

Selenium may also play a role in cancer. Animal and epidemiological studies have suggested there may be an inverse relationship between selenium supplementation and cancer risk.[[7](#cit/section_7.7)] The Nutritional Prevention of Cancer Trial (NPC) was a [randomized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [placebo-controlled](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44840&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study designed to test the [hypothesis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44209&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that higher selenium levels were associated with lower [incidence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46145&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of [skin cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445084&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The results indicated that selenium supplementation did not affect risk of skin cancer, although incidences of [lung](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445043&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [colorectal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=444983&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and prostate cancer were significantly reduced.[[8](#cit/section_7.8)]

There is evidence that selenoproteins may be associated with [carcinogenesis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). For example, reduced expression of glutathione peroxidase 3 and SEPP have been observed in some [tumors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), while increased expression of glutathione peroxidase 2 occurs in colorectal and lung tumors.[[7](#cit/section_7.7)]

Some companies distribute selenium as a [dietary supplement](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. The FDA can remove dietary supplements from the market that are deemed unsafe. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of selenium as a treatment or prevention for cancer.

### Preclinical/Animal Studies

#### *In vitro* studies

Different selenium-containing [compounds](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=422394&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have variable effects on prostate cancer [cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) as well as normal cells and [tissues](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46683&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Both naturally occurring and [synthetic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=462950&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) organic forms of selenium have been shown to decrease the growth and function of prostate cancer cells.[[9](#cit/section_7.9)] In a 2011 study, prostate cancer cells were treated with various forms of selenium; selenite and methylseleninic [acid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=642987&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (MSeA) had the greatest [cytotoxic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44020&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects.[[10](#cit/section_7.10)]

Studies have suggested that selenium [nanoparticles](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653131&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may be less [toxic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43986&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) to normal tissues than are other selenium compounds. One study investigated the effects of selenium nanoparticles on prostate cancer cells. The treated cells had decreased activity of the [androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [receptor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44958&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), which led to [apoptosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46524&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and growth inhibition.[[11](#cit/section_7.11)]

##### Sodium selenite

In a 2010 study, prostate cancer cells treated with [sodium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44731&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) selenite (a natural form of selenium) exhibited increased levels of [p53](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45813&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (a [tumor suppressor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46657&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic)). Findings also revealed that p53 may play a key role in selenium-induced apoptosis.[[12](#cit/section_7.12)]

In a second study, the hormone-sensitive prostate [cancer cell line](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=695994&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) LNCaP was modified to separately [overexpress](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45812&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) each of four antioxidant enzymes. Cells from the modified cell line were then treated with sodium selenite. The cells overexpressing manganese superoxide dismutase (*MnSOD*) were the only ones able to suppress selenite-induced apoptosis. These findings suggest that superoxide production in [mitochondria](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44060&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may be important in selenium-induced apoptosis occurring in prostate cancer cells and that levels of *MnSOD* in cancer cells may determine the effectiveness of selenium in inhibiting those cells.[[13](#cit/section_7.13)]

One study treated prostate cancer cells and [benign prostatic hyperplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46509&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (BPH) cells with sodium selenite. Growth of LNCaP cells was stimulated by noncytotoxic, low concentrations of sodium selenite; while growth inhibition occurred in hormone-insensitive PC-3 cells at these concentrations—prompting the authors to suggest that selenium may be beneficial in advanced prostate cancer—selenium supplementation may have [adverse effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44922&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in hormone-sensitive prostate cancer.[[14](#cit/section_7.14)] However, the relevance of these findings to the [clinical](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44168&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) setting is unclear. These experiments used selenium concentrations of 1 µg/[mL](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655103&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) to 10 µg/mL, whereas the average U.S. adult male [serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) selenium concentrations are about 0.125 µg/mL,[[15](#cit/section_7.15)] and prostate tissue concentrations are about 1.5 µg/g.[[16](#cit/section_7.16)]

#### Animal studies

A 2012 study investigated whether various forms of selenium (i.e., SeMet and selenium-enriched yeast [Se-yeast]) differentially affect [biomarkers](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45618&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in the prostate. Elderly dogs received nutritionally adequate or supranutritional levels of selenium in the form of SeMet or Se-yeast. Both types of selenium supplementation increased selenium levels in toenails and prostate tissue to a similar degree. The different forms of selenium supplementation showed no significant differences in [DNA](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45671&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) damage, proliferation, or apoptosis in the prostate.[[17](#cit/section_7.17)]

At least one study has compared these three forms of selenium in [athymic nude mice](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44579&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [injected](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44678&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with human prostate cancer cells and found that MSeA was more effective in inhibiting tumor growth than was SeMet or selenite.[[18](#cit/section_7.18)] Another study investigated the effect of age on selenium [chemoprevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in mice. Mice were fed selenium-depleted or selenium-containing (at nutritional or supranutritional levels) [diets](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for 6 months or 4 weeks and were then injected with PC-3 prostate cancer cells. Adult mice that were fed selenium-containing diets exhibited fewer tumors than did adult mice fed selenium-depleted diets. In adult mice, selenium-depleted diets resulted in tumors with more [necrosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44290&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [inflammation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) compared with selenium-containing diets. However, in young mice, tumor development and [histopathology](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467841&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were not affected by dietary selenium.[[19](#cit/section_7.19)]

The effects of MSeA and methylselenocysteine (MSeC) have also been explored in a transgenic model of *[in situ](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=638205&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* murine prostate cancer development, the transgenic adenocarcinoma of the mouse prostate (TRAMP) mouse.[[20](#cit/section_7.20)] Treatment with MSeA and MSeC resulted in slower [progression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44078&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of [prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44345&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (PIN) lesions, decreased [cell proliferation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46479&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and increased apoptosis compared with treatment with water. MSeA treatment also increased survival time of TRAMP mice. TRAMP mice that received MSeA treatment starting at age 10 weeks exhibited less [aggressive](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46053&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer than did mice that started treatment at 16 weeks, suggesting early [intervention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454757&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with MSeA may be more effective than later treatment. The same research group later investigated some of the cellular mechanisms responsible for the different effects of MSeA and MSeC. MSeA and MSeC were shown to affect proteins involved in different cellular pathways. MSeA mainly affected proteins related to prostate [differentiation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46445&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), androgen receptor signaling, protein folding, and endoplasmic reticulum-stress responses, whereas MSeC affected enzymes involved in [phase II detoxification](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390310&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or cytoprotection.[[21](#cit/section_7.21)] One study suggested that MSeA may inhibit cell growth and increase apoptosis by inactivating PKC isoenzymes.[[22](#cit/section_7.22)]

### Human Studies

#### Epidemiological studies

The results of [epidemiological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies suggest some complexity in the association between the blood levels of selenium and the risk of acquiring prostate cancer. As part of the European Prospective Investigation into Cancer and Nutrition (EPIC)-Heidelberg study, men completed dietary questionnaires, had blood samples taken, and were monitored every 2 to 3 years for up to 10 years. The findings revealed a significantly decreased risk of prostate cancer for individuals with higher blood selenium concentrations.[[23](#cit/section_7.23)] In a [prospective](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [pilot study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45838&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), prostate cancer patients had significantly lower whole blood selenium levels than did healthy males.[[24](#cit/section_7.24)] However, in a 2009 study of prostate cancer patients, men with higher [plasma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45839&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) selenium levels were at greater risk of being [diagnosed](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46450&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with aggressive prostate cancer ([relative risk](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=618613&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 1.35; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.99–1.84).[[25](#cit/section_7.25)]

Various molecular pathways have been explored to better understand the association between blood selenium levels and the development of prostate cancer. In the EPIC-Heidelberg study, [polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44805&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in the selenium-containing enzymes *GPX1* and *SEP15* [genes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) were found to be associated with prostate cancer risk.[[23](#cit/section_7.23)] Another study that used DNA samples obtained from the EPIC-Heidelberg study suggested that prostate cancer risk may be associated with [single nucleotide polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=458046&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) (SNPs) in thioredoxin reductase and selenoprotein K genes along with selenium status.[[26](#cit/section_7.26)] A 2012 study investigated associations between variants in selenoenzyme genes and risk of prostate cancer and prostate cancer–specific [mortality](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=496502&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Among SNPs analyzed, only *GPX1* rs3448 was related to overall prostate cancer risk.[[27](#cit/section_7.27)]

A [retrospective](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44120&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390238&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer patients and healthy controls showed an association between aggressive prostate cancer and decreased selenium and *SEPP* status.[[28](#cit/section_7.28)] In the Physicians' Health Study, links between SNPs in the *SEPP* gene (*SEPP1*) and prostate cancer risk and survival were examined. Two SNPs were significantly associated with prostate cancer incidence: rs11959466 was associated with increased risk, and rs13168440 was associated with decreased risk. Tumor *SEPP1* [mRNA](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=662001&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) expression levels were lower in men with lethal prostate cancer than in men with nonlethal prostate cancer.[[29](#cit/section_7.29)] In one study, the direction of the association between blood selenium levels and advanced prostate cancer incidence differed according to which of two polymorphisms a patient had for the gene encoding the enzyme MnSOD. For men with the alanine-alanine (AA) [genotype](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=660739&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic), higher selenium levels were associated with a reduced risk of presenting with aggressive disease, whereas the opposite was seen among men with a valine (V) [allele](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=339337&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic).[[25](#cit/section_7.25)]

An analysis of 4,459 men in the Health Professionals Follow-Up Study who were initially diagnosed with prostate cancer found that selenium supplementation of 140 μg or more per day after [diagnosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46450&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of [nonmetastatic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45805&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer may increase risk of prostate cancer mortality. The authors recommended caution in the use of selenium supplements among men with prostate cancer. Risk of prostate cancer mortality rose at all levels of selenium consumption. Men who consumed 1 to 24 μg/day, 25 to 139 μg/day, and 140 μg/day or more of supplemental selenium had a 1.18-fold (95% CI, 0.73–1.91), 1.33-fold (95% CI, 0.77–2.30), and 2.60-fold (95% CI, 1.44–4.70) increased prostate cancer mortality risk compared with nonusers, respectively (*P*trend = .001). The authors reported no statistically significant association between selenium supplement use and [biochemical recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=543628&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [cardiovascular disease](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=748137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) mortality, or overall mortality.[[30](#cit/section_7.30)]

In summary, these epidemiological studies present a conflicting picture. Some studies showed that higher selenium levels were associated with a decreased risk of prostate cancer; others showed a correlation between higher selenium levels and more aggressive prostate cancer. [Genetic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46391&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) differences in the *SEPP* gene may explain the different responses to selenium.

#### Interventional studies

Interventional studies have examined the [efficacy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=346517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of selenium in preventing and treating prostate cancer.

##### Prevention

In one study, 60 healthy adult males were randomly assigned to receive either a daily [placebo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46688&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or 200 µg of selenium glycinate supplements for 6 weeks. Blood samples were collected at the start and end of the study. Compared with the placebo group, men who received selenium supplements had significantly increased activities of two blood selenium enzymes and significantly decreased levels of [prostate-specific antigen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46540&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (PSA) at the end of the study.[[31](#cit/section_7.31)]

A [meta-analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) published in 2012 reviewed human studies that investigated links between selenium intake, selenium status, and prostate cancer risk. The results suggested an association between decreased prostate cancer risk and a narrow range of selenium status (plasma selenium concentrations up to 170 ng/mL and toenail selenium concentrations between 0.85 and 0.94 µg/g).[[32](#cit/section_7.32)]

However, in 2013, results of a [phase III](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45833&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) randomized, placebo-controlled trial were reported. The trial investigated the effect of selenium supplementation on prostate cancer incidence in men at high risk for the disease. Participants (N = 699) were randomly assigned to receive either daily placebo or one of two [doses](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44664&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of high–Se-yeast (200 µg/d or 400 µg/d). The participants were monitored every 6 months for up to 5 years. Compared with placebo, selenium supplementation had no effect on prostate cancer incidence or [PSA velocity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651206&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[33](#cit/section_7.33)] Another study examined men with high-grade prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia (HGPIN) who were randomly assigned to receive either placebo or 200 µg of selenium daily for 3 years or until prostate cancer diagnosis. The results also suggested that selenium supplementation had no effect on prostate cancer risk.[[34](#cit/section_7.34)]

A 2018 Cochrane review that examined the role of selenium in cancer prevention consolidated these studies in a meta-analysis and noted a risk ratio of 1.01 (95% CI, 0.90–1.14) when four prostate cancer studies were reviewed that involved 18,942 patients.[[35](#cit/section_7.35)]

###### The Selenium and Vitamin E Cancer Prevention Trial (SELECT)

On the basis of findings from earlier studies,[[8](#cit/section_7.8),[36](#cit/section_7.36)] the SELECT, a large multicenter clinical trial, was initiated by the [National Institutes of Health](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44268&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in 2001 to examine the effects of selenium and/or vitamin E on the development of prostate cancer. SELECT was a phase III, randomized, [double-blind](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), placebo-controlled, population-based trial.[[37](#cit/section_7.37)] More than 35,000 men, aged 50 years or older, from more than 400 study sites in the United States, Canada, and Puerto Rico, were randomly assigned to receive vitamin E ([alpha-tocopherol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691423&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [acetate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655020&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 400 [IU](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44764&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/d) and a placebo, selenium (L-selenomethionine, 200 µg/d) and a placebo, vitamin E and selenium, or two placebos daily for 7 to 12 years. The [primary endpoint](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44163&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the clinical trial was incidence of prostate cancer.[[37](#cit/section_7.37)]

Initial results of SELECT were published in 2009. There were no statistically significant differences in rates of prostate cancer in the four groups. In the vitamin E–alone group, there was a nonsignificant increase in rates of prostate cancer (*P* = .06); in the selenium–alone group, there was a nonsignificant increase in incidence of [diabetes mellitus](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44129&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (*P* = .16). On the basis of those findings, the data and safety monitoring committee recommended that participants stop taking the study supplements.[[38](#cit/section_7.38)]

Updated results of SELECT were published in 2011. When compared with the placebo group, the rate of prostate cancer detection was significantly higher in the vitamin E–alone group (*P* = .008) and represented a 17% increase in prostate cancer risk. The incidence of prostate cancer was also higher in men who took selenium than in men who took placebo, but the differences were not statistically significant.[[39](#cit/section_7.39)]

A number of explanations have been suggested, including the dose and form of vitamin E used in the trial and the specific form of selenium chosen for the study. L-selenomethionine was used in SELECT, while selenite and Se-yeast had been used in previous studies. SELECT researchers chose selenomethionine because it was the major component of Se-yeast and because selenite was not absorbed well by the body, resulting in lower selenium stores.[[40](#cit/section_7.40)] In addition, there were concerns about product consistency with high–Se-yeast.[[41](#cit/section_7.41)] However, selenomethionine is involved in general protein synthesis and can have numerous metabolites such as methylselenol, which may have [antitumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=446109&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) properties.[[42](#cit/section_7.42),[43](#cit/section_7.43)]

Toenail selenium concentrations were examined in two-case cohort subset studies of SELECT participants. Total selenium concentration in the absence of supplementation was not associated with prostate cancer risk. Although selenium supplementation in SELECT had no effect on prostate cancer risk among men with low selenium status at baseline, it increased the risk of high-grade prostate cancer in men with higher baseline selenium status by 91% (*P* = .007). The authors concluded that men should avoid selenium supplementation at doses exceeding recommended dietary intakes.[[44](#cit/section_7.44)]

Complicating this picture, an international collaboration compiled and reanalyzed data from 15 studies, including the SELECT trial, that investigated the association between blood and toenail selenium concentrations and prostate cancer risk.[[45](#cit/section_7.45)] In the analysis of 6,497 men with prostate cancer and 8,107 controls, blood selenium level was not associated with the risk of total prostate cancer, but high blood selenium level was associated with a lower risk of aggressive disease. Toenail selenium concentration was inversely associated with risk of total prostate cancer ([odds ratio](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=618610&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 0.29; 95% CI, 0.22–0.40; *P*trend < .001), including both aggressive and nonaggressive disease.

In a case-cohort analysis of the SELECT trial, 1,434 men underwent analysis of SNPs in 21 genes, investigators found support for the hypothesis that [genetic variation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=776887&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in selenium and vitamin E metabolism/transport genes may influence the risk of overall and high-grade prostate cancer; selenium or vitamin E supplementation may modify an individual's response to those risks.[[46](#cit/section_7.46)]

In summary, data from the SELECT trial did not provide evidence that selenium, when given to unselected populations, decreased the risk of prostate cancer. Subsequent analyses have shown that baseline selenium levels may influence the outcomes of selenium supplementation, though the evidence remains conflicting. Emerging evidence suggests that SNPs in genes related to both selenium and prostate cancer likely modify the effect of selenium supplementation. Further research is needed to better understand which patients may benefit from or be harmed by selenium supplementation.

To date, the most recent literature demonstrates that when administered to a non-selected population, selenium has no significant effect on either prostate cancer prevention or PSA levels.

##### Treatment

A study explored the potential role of selenium in prostate cancer patients on [active surveillance](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=616060&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). It examined 140 men who were randomly assigned to receive low-dose selenium (200 µg/d), high-dose selenium (800 µg/d), or placebo daily for up to 5 years. Selenium was given in the form of Se-yeast. The results showed no significant difference in PSA velocity across treatment groups. Concerningly, men who received high-dose selenium and had the highest baseline plasma selenium levels, had a higher [PSA velocity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651206&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) than did men in the placebo group. There was no significant effect of selenium supplements on PSA velocity in men who had lower baseline levels of selenium.[[47](#cit/section_7.47)]

Another study examined the potential role selenium played in the adjuvant setting. Prostate cancer patients were randomly assigned to receive either combination [silymarin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=340936&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (570 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) and selenomethionine (240 µg) supplement or placebo daily for 6 months following [radical prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46549&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). While there was no change in PSA levels between the groups after 6 months, the participants who received supplements reported improved [quality of life](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45417&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and showed decreases in low-density lipoprotein cholesterol and total cholesterol.[[48](#cit/section_7.48)]

#### Current Clinical Trials

Use our [advanced clinical trial search](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced") to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. [General information](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials") about clinical trials is also available.

### Adverse Effects

Selenium supplementation was well tolerated in many clinical trials. In two published trials, there were no differences reported in adverse effects between placebo or treatment groups.[[33](#cit/section_7.33),[47](#cit/section_7.47)] However, in SELECT, selenium supplementation was associated with a nonsignificant increase in incidence of diabetes mellitus (*P* = .08).[[38](#cit/section_7.38)]

## Soy

### Overview

This section contains the following key information:

- [Soy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407766&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) foods (e.g., soy milk, miso, tofu, and soy flour) contain [phytochemicals](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44709&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that may have health benefits and, among these, soy [isoflavones](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have been the focus of most of the research.
- Soy isoflavones are [phytoestrogens](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=330175&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The major isoflavones in [soybeans](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=415913&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) are [genistein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46107&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (the most abundant), [daidzein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285740&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and glycitein.
- Genistein affects components of multiple growth and [proliferation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44572&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-related pathways in [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), including the [COX-2](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=589403&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/[prostaglandin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=559143&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [epidermal growth factor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653114&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (EGF), and [insulin-like growth factor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653119&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (IGF) pathways.
- Some [preclinical studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have indicated that the combined effect of multiple isoflavones may be greater than that of a single isoflavone.
- Some [animal studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454774&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have demonstrated prostate cancer [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects with soy and genistein; however, other animal studies have yielded conflicting results regarding beneficial effects of genistein on prostate cancer [metastasis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46710&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- [Epidemiological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies have generally found high consumption of nonfermented soy foods to be associated with a decreased risk of prostate cancer.
- Early-phase [clinical trials](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45961&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with isoflavones, soy, and soy products for the prevention and treatment of prostate cancer have been limited to relatively short durations of intervention and sample sizes with low statistical power. These studies targeted [heterogeneous](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44807&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer patient populations (in high-risk, early- and later-[stage](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45885&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) disease) and varying doses of isoflavones, soy, and soy products, and have not demonstrated evidence of reducing prostate cancer [progression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44078&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Other trials evaluating the role of isoflavones, soy, or soy products in the management of [androgen deprivation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=559086&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) therapy (ADT) [side effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46580&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have found no improvement with isoflavone treatment compared with placebo.
- Soy products are generally well tolerated in patients with prostate cancer. In clinical trials, the most commonly reported side effects were mild [gastrointestinal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45692&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) symptoms.

### General Information and History

Soybean, a major food source and a medicinal substance, has been used in China for centuries. Soybean was used as one of the early food sources in China.[[1](#cit/section_8.1),[2](#cit/section_8.2)] Soybean was mentioned in the book titled, *The Classic of Poetry* (Shijing, 11th–7th centuries BCE), with its collection and cultivation. During the Warring States period (475–221 BCE), soybean became one of five major foods (“five grains”) of the Chinese. The medical use of soybean was also discussed in one of the major Chinese medicine books titled, *Inner Canon of the Yellow Emperor* (Huangdi Neijing, 400 BCE and 260 BCE), which stated that “five grains are used to nourish and replenish the body." In [traditional Chinese medicine](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=449722&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), soybean has been used to treat [kidney](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46325&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [conditions](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651193&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), promote water retention and reduce swelling, and for weakness, dizziness, poor sleep, and [night sweats](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454810&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

Although records of soy use in China date back to the 11th century BCE, it was not until the 18th century that the soy plant reached Europe and the United States. The soybean is an incredibly versatile plant. It can be processed into a variety of products including soy milk, miso, tofu, soy flour, and soy oil.[[3](#cit/section_8.3)]

Soy foods contain a number of phytochemicals that may have health benefits, but isoflavones have garnered the most attention. Among the isoflavones found in soybeans, genistein is the most abundant and may have the most [biological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44510&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) activity.[[4](#cit/section_8.4)] Other isoflavones found in soy include daidzein and glycitein.[[5](#cit/section_8.5)] Many of these isoflavones are also found in other legumes and plants, such as [red clover](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=330178&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

Isoflavones are quickly taken up by the gut and can be detected in [plasma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45839&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) as soon as 30 minutes after the consumption of soy products. Studies suggest that maximum levels of isoflavone plasma [concentration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=683342&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may be achieved by 6 hours after soy product consumption.[[6](#cit/section_8.6)] Isoflavones are phytoestrogens that bind to [estrogen receptors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46409&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Prostate [tissue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46683&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is known to express estrogen receptor beta and it has been shown that the isoflavone genistein has greater [affinity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655052&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for estrogen receptor beta than for estrogen receptor alpha.[[7](#cit/section_8.7)]

A link between isoflavones and prostate cancer was first observed in epidemiological studies that demonstrated a lower risk of prostate cancer in populations consuming considerable amounts of dietary soy.[[8](#cit/section_8.8),[9](#cit/section_8.9)] Subsequent studies evaluating the role of soy in experimental models further showed anticancer properties of soy, specifically relevant to prostate [carcinogenesis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). These early studies have led to a few clinical trials in humans using soy food products or supplements that targeted men with varying stages of prostate cancer. Although these studies showed modulation of intermediate [endpoints](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=346519&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or surrogate [biomarkers](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45618&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer progression, the results indicating beneficial effects from soy or soy products have been mixed.

Several companies distribute soy as a [dietary supplement](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease prevention or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. The FDA can remove dietary supplements from the market that are deemed unsafe. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of soy as a treatment for [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or any other medical condition.

### Preclinical/Animal Studies

#### *In vitro* studies

##### Individual isoflavones

A number of [laboratory studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44512&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have examined ways in which soy components affect prostate cancer cells. In one study, human prostate cancer cells and normal prostate [epithelial](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45682&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) cells were treated with either an ethanol vehicle (carrier) or isoflavones. Treatment with genistein decreased COX-2 [mRNA](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=662001&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [protein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46092&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels in cancer cells and normal epithelial cells more than did treatment with the vehicle. In addition, cells treated with genistein exhibited reduced [secretion](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=643082&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostaglandin E2 (PGE2) and reduced mRNA levels of the prostaglandin [receptors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44958&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) EP4 and FP, suggesting that genistein may exert [chemopreventive](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects by inhibiting the synthesis of prostaglandins, which promote [inflammation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[10](#cit/section_8.10)] In another study, human prostate cancer cells were treated with genistein or daidzein. The isoflavones were shown to down regulate [growth factors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45705&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) involved in [angiogenesis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46529&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (e.g., [EGF](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653115&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and IGF-1) and the [interleukin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46069&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-8 [gene](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic), which is associated with cancer progression. These findings suggest that genistein and daidzein may have chemopreventive properties.[[11](#cit/section_8.11)] Both genistein and daidzein have been shown to reduce the proliferation of LNCaP and PC-3 prostate cancer cells *[in vitro](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)*. However, during the 72 hours of [incubation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45734&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), only genistein provoked effects on the dynamic [phenotype](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460203&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) and decreased invasiveness in PC-3 cells. These results imply that invasive activity is at least partially dependent on [membrane](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46294&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) fluidity and that genistein may exert its antimetastatic effects by changing the mechanical properties of prostate cancer cells. No such effects were observed for daidzein at the same dose.[[12](#cit/section_8.12)]

##### Combinations of isoflavones

Some experiments have compared the effects of individual isoflavones with isoflavone combinations on prostate cancer cells. In one study, human prostate cancer cells were treated with a soy [extract](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407760&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (containing genistin, daidzein, and glycitin), genistein, or daidzein. The soy extract induced [cell cycle](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=597111&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) arrest and [apoptosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46524&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in prostate cancer cells to a greater degree than did treatment with the individual isoflavones. Genistein and daidzein activated apoptosis in noncancerous [benign prostatic hyperplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46509&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (BPH) cells, but the soy extract had no effect on those cells. These findings suggested that products containing a combination of active [compounds](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=422394&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (e.g., *whole foods*) may be more effective in preventing cancer than individual compounds.[[13](#cit/section_8.13)] Similarly, in another study, prostate cancer cells were treated with genistein, [biochanin A](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44148&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), quercetin, doublets of those compounds (e.g., genistein + quercetin), or with all three compounds. All of the treatments resulted in decreased cell proliferation, but the greatest reductions occurred using the combination of genistein, biochanin A, and quercetin. The triple combination treatment induced more apoptosis in prostate cancer cells than did individual or doublet compound treatments. These results indicate that combining phytoestrogens may increase the effectiveness of the individual compounds.[[14](#cit/section_8.14)]

At least one study has examined the combined effect of soy isoflavones and [curcumin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46049&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Human prostate cancer cells were treated with isoflavones, curcumin, or a combination of the two. Curcumin and isoflavones in combination were more effective in lowering PSA levels and expression of the [androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) receptor than were curcumin or the isoflavones individually.[[15](#cit/section_8.15)]

#### Animal studies

[Animal models](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43996&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer have been used in studies investigating the effects of soy and isoflavones on the disease. Wild-type and [transgenic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691466&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [adenocarcinoma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46216&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the mouse prostate (TRAMP) mice were fed control diets or diets containing genistein (250 mg genistein/kg chow). The TRAMP mice fed with genistein exhibited reduced cell proliferation in the prostate compared with TRAMP mice fed a control diet. The genistein-supplemented diet also reduced levels of ERK-1 and ERK-2 (proteins important in stimulating cell proliferation) as well as the growth factor receptors epidermal growth factor receptor (EGFR) and insulin like growth factor-1 receptor (IGF-1R) in TRAMP mice, suggesting that down regulation of these proteins may be one mechanism by which genistein exerts chemopreventive effects.[[16](#cit/section_8.16)] In one study, following the appearance of spontaneous [prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44345&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [lesions](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46324&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), TRAMP mice were fed control diets or diets supplemented with genistein (250 or 1,000 mg genistein/kg chow). Mice fed low-dose genistein exhibited more cancer cell metastasis and greater osteopontin expression than mice fed the control or the high-dose genistein diet. These results indicate that timing and dose of genistein treatment may affect prostate cancer outcomes and that genistein may exert biphasic control over prostate cancer.[[17](#cit/section_8.17)]

In a study reported in 2008, athymic mice were implanted with human prostate cancer cells and fed a control or genistein-supplemented diet (100 or 250 mg genistein/kg chow). Mice that were fed genistein exhibited less cancer cell metastasis but no change in primary tumor volume, compared with mice fed a control diet. Furthermore, other data suggested that genistein inhibits metastasis by impairing cancer cell detachment.[[18](#cit/section_8.18)]

In contrast, in a study reported in 2011, there were more metastases in secondary [organs](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257523&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in genistein-treated mice than in vehicle-treated mice. In this latter study, mice were implanted with human prostate cancer [xenografts](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44095&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and treated daily with genistein dissolved in peanut oil (80 mg genistein/kg body weight/d or 400 mg genistein/kg body weight/d) or peanut oil vehicle by [gavage](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=729771&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In addition, there was a reduction in tumor cell apoptosis in the genistein-treated mice compared with the vehicle-treated mice. These findings suggest that genistein may stimulate metastasis in an animal model of [advanced](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=478743&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer.[[19](#cit/section_8.19)]

[Radiation therapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44971&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is commonly used in prostate cancer, but, despite this treatment, disease [recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is common. Therefore, combining radiation with additional therapies may provide longer-lasting results. In one study, human prostate cancer cells were treated with soy isoflavones and/or radiation. Cells that were treated with both isoflavones and radiation exhibited greater decreases in cell survival and greater expression of proapoptotic [molecules](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45065&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) than cells treated with isoflavones or radiation only. Nude mice were implanted with prostate cancer cells and treated by gavage with genistein (21.5 mg/kg body weight/d), mixed isoflavones (50 mg/kg body weight/d; contained 43% genistein, 21% daidzein, and 2% glycitein), and/or radiation. Mixed isoflavones were more effective than genistein in inhibiting prostate tumor growth, and combining isoflavones with radiation resulted in the largest inhibition of tumor growth. In addition, mice given soy isoflavones in combination with radiation did not exhibit [lymph node](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45762&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) metastasis, which was seen previously in other experiments combining genistein with radiation. These preclinical findings suggest that mixed isoflavones may increase the efficacy of radiation therapy for prostate cancer.[[20](#cit/section_8.20)]

In the treatment of prostate cancer, bone health is a common concern in the setting of [hormone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45713&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) deprivation therapy, which is associated with bone loss. Because of increased beta versus alpha estrogen receptor binding, soy-derived compounds are thought to be protective of bone. Animal studies have shown that genistein and daidzein can prevent or reduce bone loss in a manner similar to [synthetic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=462950&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) estrogen. Both isoflavones may [modulate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390300&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) bone remodeling by targeting and regulating [gene expression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=537335&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and may inhibit [calcium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45632&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [urine](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46642&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) excretion, which also helps to maintain [bone density](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407755&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[21](#cit/section_8.21),[22](#cit/section_8.22)]

### Human Studies

Human studies evaluating isoflavones and soy for the prevention and treatment of prostate cancer have included epidemiological studies and early-phase trials. Several phase I-II [randomized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) clinical studies have examined isoflavones and soy product for [bioavailability](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), safety, and effectiveness in prostate cancer prevention or treatment.[[23](#cit/section_8.23)-[25](#cit/section_8.25)] These studies have included a wide range of subject populations, including high-risk men; prostate cancer patient populations ([localized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45754&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and later-stage disease); varying doses of isoflavones, soy, and soy products; and were limited to relatively short durations of [observation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45981&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or intervention and sample sizes with low statistical power.

#### Epidemiological studies

In 2018, a [meta-analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of studies that investigated soy food consumption and risk of prostate cancer was reported. The results of this meta-analysis suggested that high consumption of nonfermented soy foods (e.g., tofu and soybean milk) was significantly associated with a decrease in the risk of prostate cancer. Fermented soy food intake, total isoflavone intake, and circulating isoflavones were not associated with a reduced risk of prostate cancer.[[26](#cit/section_8.26)] However, these data from [population studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=561718&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) must be interpreted with caution as the studies relied on self-reported data obtained using varying forms of dietary data collection instruments with recall [bias](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44646&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), in addition to numerous forms of individual or multiple isoflavones, soy supplements, and soy foods. Additionally, these studies failed to account for other confounding [genetic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46391&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or behavioral variables that may affect the risk of prostate cancer.

#### Prevention studies

Too few randomized [placebo-controlled](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44840&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) trials have been completed to evaluate the effect of isoflavones or soy in preventing prostate cancer progression (see [Table 3](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_704)). The studies targeted men with negative prostate biopsies and elevated [serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate-specific antigen (PSA) (2.5–10 mcg/mL at [baseline](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)). The duration of intervention was between 6 months [[15](#cit/section_8.15)] and 1 year [[27](#cit/section_8.27),[28](#cit/section_8.28)], with varying formulations of isoflavones derived from soy [[15](#cit/section_8.15),[27](#cit/section_8.27)] and red clover.[[28](#cit/section_8.28)] In a single trial that showed no significant changes in serum PSA after intervention with isoflavones, a reduction in prostate cancer progression at 1 year in a subgroup of men older than 65 years was demonstrated. Other than mild to moderate adverse events, no treatment-related [toxicities](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445093&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were observed in all three trials.

#### Treatment of prostate cancer

Clinical trials evaluating isoflavones, soy supplements, and soy products (see [Table 4](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_705) and [Table 5](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_706)) for treating localized prostate cancer before [radical prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46549&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) have used window-of-opportunity trial designs (from biopsy to prostatectomy). These trials have primarily focused on evaluating serum and tissue biomarkers implicated in prostate cancer progression, bioavailability in plasma and prostate tissue, and toxicity at various doses. The trials are small in size and of short duration. They are useful for informing the design of well-powered larger clinical trials in the future, but they provide inadequate data to inform clinical practice.

#### Isoflavones and soy products for biochemical recurrence after treatment

Other studies have examined the role of isoflavones and soy products in prostate cancer patients with [biochemical recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=543628&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) after treatment. However, these early-phase studies have not demonstrated any significant changes in serum PSA or PSA-doubling time, [[40](#cit/section_8.40)-[43](#cit/section_8.43)] with one study suggesting modulation of [systemic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45921&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [soluble](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and cellular biomarkers consistent with limiting inflammation and suppression of myeloid-derived suppressor cells [[43](#cit/section_8.43)] (see [Table 6](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_707)).

Table 6. Clinical Trials of Soy and Soy Products in Men on Active Surveillance or With Biochemical Recurrence After Treatment for Prostate Cancer

| Reference | Trial Design | Dose | Duration of Intervention | Treatment Group (Enrolled; Treated; Placebo or No Treatment Control) | Toxicities | Results | Levels of Evidencea |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| GCP = genistein combined polysaccharide; GI = gastrointestinal; PCa = prostate cancer; RCT = randomized controlled trial. | | | | | | | |
| aStrongest evidence reported that the treatment under study has activity or improves the well-being of cancer patients. For information about levels of evidence analysis and scores, see [Levels of Evidence for Human Studies of Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies](/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/cam). | | | | | | | |
| [[40](#cit/section_8.40)] | Nonrandomized | Soy beverage daily (providing approximately 65–90 mg isoflavones) | 6 mo | 34; 29; None | Adverse events included minor GI side effects | No statistically significant findings regarding PSA, PSA-doubling time | [2C](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335134&version=HealthProfessional&language=English) |
| [[41](#cit/section_8.41)] | Open-label | Soy milk 3x/d (isoflavones, 141 mg/d) | 12 mo | 20; 20; None | Toxicity data lacks details; GI (loose [stools](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46605&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) toxicities were the most common complaint from a small number of men in the GCP group | No statistically significant findings regarding serum PSA changes | [2Dii](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335136&version=HealthProfessional&language=English) |
| [[42](#cit/section_8.42)] | RCT | Beverage powder containing soy-protein isolate (20 g protein) or calcium caseinate | 2 y | 177; 87; 90 | All adverse events were grades I-II; there were no differences in adverse events between the two groups | No significant findings regarding serum PSA changes | [1iDii](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335123&version=HealthProfessional&language=English) |
| [[43](#cit/section_8.43)] | RCT | Two slices of soy bread containing 68 mg/d soy isoflavones or soy bread containing almond powder | 56 d | 32; 25; None | Soy and soy-almond breads were without grade 2 or higher toxicity | Significant modulation of multiple plasma [cytokines](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46130&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and chemokines | [1iiDii](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335129&version=HealthProfessional&language=English) |

#### Management of androgen deprivation therapy side-effects

ADT is commonly used for [locally advanced](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45955&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and metastatic prostate cancer. However, this treatment is associated with a number of adverse side effects including sexual [dysfunction](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390268&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), decreased [quality of life](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45417&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), changes in [cognition](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=476290&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and [metabolic syndrome](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=643126&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Three studies have examined men undergoing ADT who were randomly assigned to receive a placebo or an isoflavone supplement (soy protein powder mixed with beverages; isoflavones, 160 mg/d) for 12 weeks. Two studies assessed ADT side effects. Neither study found an improvement in side effects following isoflavone treatment, compared with placebo.[[44](#cit/section_8.44),[45](#cit/section_8.45)]

The third randomized placebo-controlled trial assessed changes in PSA level and biomarkers of energy [metabolism](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46173&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (e.g., [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [glucose](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44033&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) level) and inflammation (e.g., blood [interleukin-6](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44421&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) level). In this study of men undergoing ADT, participants were randomly assigned to receive high-dose isoflavone supplements (providing 160 mg/d total isoflavones, and containing 64 mg genistein, 63 mg daidzein, and 34 mg glycitein) or a placebo for 12 weeks. The results showed no difference between the two groups in PSA levels or in levels of [metabolic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44056&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [inflammatory](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373080&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) parameters (e.g., glucose, interleukin-6).[[38](#cit/section_8.38)]

#### Current Clinical Trials

Use our [advanced clinical trial search](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced") to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. [General information](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials") about clinical trials is also available.

### Adverse Effects

Overall, isoflavones, soy, and soy products were well tolerated in clinical trials of high-risk prostate cancer patients.[[28](#cit/section_8.28),[31](#cit/section_8.31),[37](#cit/section_8.37),[41](#cit/section_8.41),[44](#cit/section_8.44),[46](#cit/section_8.46)] The most commonly reported side effects were gastrointestinal symptoms.[[31](#cit/section_8.31),[40](#cit/section_8.40),[47](#cit/section_8.47)]
