JAMA 310 (2): 170-8, 2013. [[PUBMED Abstract]](http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=23839751&dopt=Abstract "http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=23839751&dopt=Abstract")
43. Lesinski GB, Reville PK, Mace TA, et al.: Consumption of soy isoflavone enriched bread in men with prostate cancer is associated with reduced proinflammatory cytokines and immunosuppressive cells. Cancer Prev Res (Phila) 8 (11): 1036-44, 2015. [[PUBMED Abstract]](http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=26276751&dopt=Abstract "http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=26276751&dopt=Abstract")
44. Sharma P, Wisniewski A, Braga-Basaria M, et al.: Lack of an effect of high dose isoflavones in men with prostate cancer undergoing androgen deprivation therapy. J Urol 182 (5): 2265-72, 2009. [[PUBMED Abstract]](http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=19758646&dopt=Abstract "http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=19758646&dopt=Abstract")
45. Vitolins MZ, Griffin L, Tomlinson WV, et al.: Randomized trial to assess the impact of venlafaxine and soy protein on hot flashes and quality of life in men with prostate cancer. J Clin Oncol 31 (32): 4092-8, 2013. [[PUBMED Abstract]](http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=24081940&dopt=Abstract "http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=24081940&dopt=Abstract")
46. Maskarinec G, Morimoto Y, Hebshi S, et al.: Serum prostate-specific antigen but not testosterone levels decrease in a randomized soy intervention among men. Eur J Clin Nutr 60 (12): 1423-9, 2006. [[PUBMED Abstract]](http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=16775579&dopt=Abstract "http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=16775579&dopt=Abstract")
47. deVere White RW, Hackman RM, Soares SE, et al.: Effects of a genistein-rich extract on PSA levels in men with a history of prostate cancer. Urology 63 (2): 259-63, 2004. [[PUBMED Abstract]](http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=14972467&dopt=Abstract "http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&db=PubMed&list_uids=14972467&dopt=Abstract")

## Vitamin D

### Overview

- [Vitamin D](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=427253&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is made naturally by the body when exposed to sunlight.
- [Preclinical studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) suggest that vitamin D may have effects on [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) through various pathways.
- Numerous [epidemiological](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies have researched the relationship between vitamin D and prostate cancer.
- Some [intervention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454757&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies have focused on [calcitriol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45408&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), the hormonally active form of vitamin D, in prostate cancer patients.

### General Information and History

Vitamin D, also called calciferol, [cholecalciferol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=427252&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (D3), or [ergocalciferol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655060&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (D2), is a [fat-soluble vitamin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=560348&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) found in fatty fish, fish liver oil, eggs, and fortified dairy products. Vitamin D is made naturally by the body when exposed to sunlight.

In 1922, researchers discovered that heated, oxidized cod-liver oil, called *fat-soluble factor A* and later known as vitamin D, played an important role in [curing](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=318813&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [rickets](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655123&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in rats.[[1](#cit/section_9.1)]

Vitamin D performs many roles in the body, including the following:

- Promotes [absorption](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463702&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of [calcium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45632&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in the [small intestine](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46582&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Improves muscle strength and [immune function](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45721&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Helps to reduce [inflammation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Helps to maintain adequate [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels of calcium and [phosphate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=476349&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

Vitamin D is needed for bone growth and protects against [osteoporosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45371&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in adults.[[2](#cit/section_9.2)] Vitamin D status is usually checked by measuring the level of 25-hydroxyvitamin D (25(OH)D) in the blood.

Companies distribute vitamin D as a [dietary supplement](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. The FDA can remove dietary supplements from the market that are deemed unsafe. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of vitamin D as a treatment for [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

### Preclinical/Animal Studies

#### *In vitro* studies

To study the role of vitamin D in cancer cell adhesion to endothelium, one study developed a microtube system that simulates the microvasculature of [bone marrow](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45622&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The study reported that 1,25-alpha-dihydroxyvitamin D3 (1,25-D3) suppressed adhesion of prostate cancer cells in the microtube system. In addition, it was shown that 1,25-D3 increased E-cadherin expression, which may prevent prostate cancer cell adhesion to endothelium by promoting cancer cell aggregation.[[3](#cit/section_9.3)]

Vitamin D–binding [protein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46092&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (VDBP) transports vitamin D in the bloodstream. Studies have shown that one of its products, VDBP-[macrophage](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44054&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) activating factor (VDBP-maf), may have [antiangiogenic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44186&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [antitumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=446109&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) activities. One study examined the effects of VDBP-maf on prostate cancer cells. Treating prostate cancer cells with VDBP-maf resulted in inhibited cellular migration, [proliferation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46479&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and reduced levels of [urokinase plasminogen activator](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=658871&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [receptor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44958&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (uPAR; activity of this receptor correlates with [tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [metastasis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46710&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)). These findings suggest that VDBP-maf has a direct effect on prostate cancer cells.[[4](#cit/section_9.4)]

Studies have reported that 1,25-D3 may play an important role in prostate cancer biology. Studies have suggested that protein disulfide isomerase family A, member 3 (PDIA3), may function as a [membrane](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46294&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) receptor binding to 1,25-D3. According to one study, PDIA3 is expressed in normal prostate cells as well as in LNCaP and PC-3 prostate cancer [cell lines](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=695994&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In addition, their findings suggest that 1,25-D3 may act on prostate cancer cells via multiple signaling pathways, indicating there may be a number of potential [therapeutic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43985&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) targets.[[5](#cit/section_9.5)]

[Androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [metabolism](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46173&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in prostate cancer cells may be altered by 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D (1,25(OH)2D), providing an additional antitumor mechanism. Vitamin D [compounds](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=422394&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [activate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43976&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [enzymes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46081&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) involved in [cholesterol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407756&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [steroid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=613764&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [hormone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45713&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) metabolism. This may reduce [intracellular](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44047&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [testosterone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45581&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels in prostate cell lines and decrease the availability of pro-survival androgenic steroids.[[6](#cit/section_9.6)]

Vitamin D has also been combined with [radiation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45072&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in an *[in vitro](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* study. In this study, prostate cancer cells were treated with [valproic acid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=367423&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (VPA) and/or 1,25-D3, followed by radiation. Cells that were treated with VPA and/or 1,25-D3 and radiation had greater decreases in cell proliferation than did cells treated solely with radiation. The greatest reduction in cell proliferation occurred in cells treated with VPA, 1,25-D3, and radiation.[[7](#cit/section_9.7)]

#### *In vivo* studies

[Tumor progression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45669&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) was compared in two murine models of prostate cancer. In vitamin D receptor–knockout animals, the rate of tumor progression and [cellular proliferation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46479&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were greater than in wild type animals. However, in mice that were supplemented with testosterone, these differences did not occur, suggesting that there may be [significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) interaction between androgen signaling and vitamin D signaling.[[8](#cit/section_9.8)]

In a 2011 study, nude mice were fed a control [diet](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or a diet deficient in vitamin D and then [injected](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44678&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with prostate cancer cells into bone marrow or [soft tissues](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45882&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). [Osteolytic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45810&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [lesions](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46324&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were larger and progressed at a faster rate in vitamin D–deficient mice that had bone marrow injected with cancer cells than in mice that had adequate levels of vitamin D. However, there was no difference in soft tissue tumors among mice with different vitamin D levels. Results of this study show that vitamin D [deficiency](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=321364&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is associated with growth of prostate cancer cells in bone but not in soft tissue.[[9](#cit/section_9.9)]

A 2014 study evaluated [calcitriol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45408&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and a less-calcemic vitamin D [analogue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44919&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in an aggressive [transgenic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691466&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [adenocarcinoma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46216&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the mouse prostate (TRAMP) model. Neither vitamin D analogue impacted the rate of development of [castration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45636&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-resistant prostate cancer in mice, whether they were treated before or after castration. However, both vitamin D analogs slowed progression of primary tumors in hormone-intact mice but enhanced distant [organ](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=257523&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) metastases after prolonged treatment. In sum, intervention with potent vitamin D compounds in TRAMP mice slowed androgen-stimulated tumor progression but, over time, may have led to more aggressive disease as indicated by increased [distant metastases](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=415317&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (*P* = .0823).[[10](#cit/section_9.10)] This preclinical data supports findings of the 2008 [retrospective study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44956&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [[11](#cit/section_9.11)] of an association between [serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) vitamin D levels and aggressive prostate cancer. For more information about this study, see the [Human Studies](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_864) section.

#### Vitamin D as adjuvant therapy

[Cryotherapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45985&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may be used for treating prostate cancer. Studies have been conducted to identify potential agents that may help improve [efficacy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=346517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the freezing procedure. In a 2010 study, mice were injected with prostate cancer cells and treated with calcitriol, [cryoablation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=518326&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), or both. The combination treatment group experienced larger necrotic areas, more [apoptosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46524&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and less cell proliferation than did the other [experimental](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44758&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) groups.[[12](#cit/section_9.12)] A subsequent study corroborated these findings, showing that combining calcitriol and cryoablation resulted in more cell death than cryotherapy alone.[[13](#cit/section_9.13)]

*In vitro* and *[in vivo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46352&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* studies have shown that vitamin D compounds potentiate the cytotoxicity of many anticancer agents, including [docetaxel](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45382&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[6](#cit/section_9.6)] The effect is most pronounced when vitamin D compounds are administered before or simultaneously with the cytotoxic agents.

### Human Studies

#### Epidemiological studies

The relationship between vitamin D and prostate cancer has been examined in numerous epidemiological studies with mixed results. A meta-analysis published in 2011 reviewed 25 studies that examined the link between prostate cancer incidence and indicators of vitamin D intake or sufficiency. No association was found between dietary vitamin D or circulating concentrations of vitamin D and risk of prostate cancer.[[14](#cit/section_9.14)]

##### Low sun exposure

An important means of obtaining vitamin D is by sunlight. Studies have investigated the potential link between sunlight exposure and prostate cancer. According to a 2006 study, [prostate-specific antigen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46540&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (PSA) levels rise at a slower rate during spring and summer than at other times of the year; this may be related to higher vitamin D levels obtained during those months.[[15](#cit/section_9.15)] One study found that while men with low levels of sun exposure had increased risk of all prostate cancers, less sun exposure was associated with lower risk of advanced disease in men with prostate cancer. Results of a meta-analysis, published in the same report, showed that men with low sun exposure had an increased risk of incident and advanced prostate cancer.[[16](#cit/section_9.16)]

##### Dietary vitamin D

The association between dietary vitamin D or circulating concentrations of vitamin D and risk of prostate cancer has been studied. In a study of 699 patients with prostate cancer who underwent [screening](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46171&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) and 958 healthy controls, calcium and vitamin D intake were evaluated using food frequency questionnaires.[[17](#cit/section_9.17)] The study population included 888 African American patients, 620 European American patients, 111 Hispanic American patients, and 38 Asian or Middle Eastern American patients. The study found that high calcium intake was significantly associated with higher odds of developing aggressive prostate cancer (odds ratio [OR] Q1 vs. Q4, 1.98; 95% confidence interval [CI], 1.01–3.91), while high vitamin D intake was associated with lower odds of developing aggressive prostate cancer (OR Q1 vs. Q4,  0.38; 95% CI, 0.18–0.79). This finding was statistically significant for African American men.

In a cross-sectional analysis of 119 men (88 African American patients and 31 European American patients) who underwent a prostatectomy, tumor proliferation (as indicated by Ki-67 measured in prostate tissue) demonstrated an inverse correlation between serum 1,25(OH)2D and Ki-67 in tumor cells. These results provided preliminary evidence of an antiproliferative activity of vitamin D. No correlation was observed between 25(OH)D and a [biomarker](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45618&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of tumor proliferation (Ki-67).[[18](#cit/section_9.18)]

##### Serum 25(OH)D

A [meta-analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of 19 [prospective](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or [cohort](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies examined the correlation between circulating 25(OH)D and the development of prostate cancer. The study explored the summary [relative risk](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=618613&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (RR) assessed per 10 ng/mL increments in circulating 25(OH)D concentration levels.[[19](#cit/section_9.19)] A higher 25(OH)D concentration was significantly correlated with an elevated risk of prostate cancer (RR, 1.15; 95% CI, 1.06–1.24).

Another meta-analysis of 19 [prospective studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) provided individual participant data on circulating 25(OH)D and 1,25(OH)2D for up to 13,462 men with incident prostate cancer and 20,261 control participants. Results showed that 25(OH)D concentration was positively associated with a risk of total prostate cancer (multivariable-adjusted OR that compared highest vs. lowest study-specific fifth, 1.22; 95% CI, 1.13–1.31; *P*trend < .001).[[20](#cit/section_9.20)] However, this association varied by disease aggressiveness (*P*heterogeneity = .014). Higher circulating 25(OH)D was associated with a higher risk of nonaggressive disease (OR per 80 percentile increase, 1.24; 95% CI, 1.13–1.36) but not with aggressive disease, defined as stage 4, metastases, or prostate cancer death (OR per 80 percentile increase, 0.95; 95% CI, 0.78–1.15). 1,25(OH)2D concentration was not associated with risk of prostate cancer overall or by tumor characteristics.[[20](#cit/section_9.20)]

In a case-control study of men who had undergone prostate [biopsies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45164&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), results showed that men who had lower vitamin D levels before biopsy were more likely to have cancer detected at biopsy than did men whose prebiopsy vitamin D levels were not lower.[[21](#cit/section_9.21)] Serum 25(OH)D levels were obtained from 667 men in Chicago who underwent a first prostate biopsy for an elevated PSA level or an abnormal digital [rectal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44083&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) exam.[[21](#cit/section_9.21)] Severe vitamin D deficiency (<12 [ng](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285977&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/[mL](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44214&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) was associated with increased risk of a prostate cancer diagnosis on biopsy among African American men. Severe deficiency was positively associated with higher Gleason score (≥4+4), higher clinical stage (>cT2b), and overall risk category in both White American and African American men.

##### Genetic factors

Investigators conducted an updated two-sample mendelian randomization analysis that examined the effect of 25(OH)D on prostate cancer.[[22](#cit/section_9.22)] Six genetic [variants](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=776887&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) associated with plasma 25(OH)D concentration were used as instrumental variables. Summary statistics for the outcome were extracted from the largest [genome-wide association study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=636779&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) to date that included 79,148 prostate cancer patients and 61,106 controls. No evidence was found to support a causal association between 25(OH)D and the risk of prostate cancer (OR per 25 nmol/L increase 1.00 [0.93–1.07]; *P*  = .99) or advanced disease (OR per 25 nmol/L increase 1.02 [0.90–1.16]; *P* = 0.72). However, even with a large number of participants, the authors stated that they could not “exclude a modest or non-linear effect of vitamin D” on the risk of prostate cancer.

Several studies have explored a possible connection between the vitamin D receptor (VDR) and risk of prostate cancer. A 2011 prospective study (N = 841) examined VDR expression in prostate tumors. Patients with high levels of VDR expression had lower PSA levels at diagnosis, less [advanced](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=478743&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) tumor stage, and reduced risk of lethal prostate cancer compared with patients with lower levels of VDR expression in tumors.[[23](#cit/section_9.23)] A 2010 study examined [polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44805&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in the VDR receptor, the vitamin D activating enzyme 1-alpha-hydroxylase (*CYP27B1*), and deactivating enzyme 24-hydroxylase (*CYP24A1*). Variations in the three [genes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) were associated with changes in the risk of [recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and progression of prostate cancer and prostate cancer mortality.[[24](#cit/section_9.24)] In a case-[controlled study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44015&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of more than 1,000 patients and controls in each group, multiple VDR [single nucleotide polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=458046&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) (SNPs) were compared with serum 25(OH)D levels. Vitamin D-related SNPs influenced serum 25(OH)D, but gene-serum 25(OH)D effect modification for prostate cancer was marginally observed only for *CYP24A1*/*rs2248359* and [high-grade](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=386205&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer. VDR correlations varied between African American and White populations.[[25](#cit/section_9.25)] In another case-controlled study, two SNPs in VDBP were associated with increased prostate cancer risk and high Gleason grade.[[26](#cit/section_9.26)] However, in another large cohort-consortium study, a statistically significant association was not observed for either 25(OH)D or vitamin D-related SNPs with fatal prostate cancer.[[27](#cit/section_9.27)]

In a 2009 study, genetic variants in VDR were analyzed in patients with prostate cancer who participated in the Prostate Testing for Cancer and Treatment (ProtecT) trial (N = 1,604). This analysis was combined with information from a meta-analysis of 13 studies. Five polymorphisms of VDR were identified in the participants. A meta-analysis, published in the same report, revealed no association between specific variants and prostate cancer stage ([TNM staging system](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=672851&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)), but found that three genotypes (*Bsm1*, *Apa1*, and *Taq1*) may be associated with cancer grade (Gleason score). This suggests that there may be a link between specific VDR polymorphisms and advanced prostate cancer at diagnosis.[[28](#cit/section_9.28)]

In a retrospective study of 515 patients with prostate cancer and an independent cohort of 411 patients, two VDR binding site variants (*HFE* and *TUSC3*) were identified as plausible susceptibility genes.[[29](#cit/section_9.29)]

A meta-analysis of 27 studies was conducted that included 9,993 prostate cancer cases and 9,345 controls. The pooled results showed that the *Bsm1* polymorphism of vitamin D metabolism was not associated with prostate cancer risk in an overall analysis.[[30](#cit/section_9.30)]

##### Survival

In a Danish Prostate Cancer Registry, a total of 4,065 men who underwent a prostate [biopsy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45164&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and had a vitamin D level checked between 2004 and 2010 were monitored.[[31](#cit/section_9.31)] No association between serum vitamin D level and prostate cancer risk was found. However, [overall survival](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655245&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) was lowest in men with serum vitamin D deficiency. A significantly higher prostate cancer–specific [mortality](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=496502&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (hazard ratio [HR], 2.37; 95% CI, 1.45–3.90; *P* < .001) and all-cause mortality (HR, 2.08; 95% CI, 1.33–3.24; *P* = .001) were observed in patients with vitamin D deficiency compared with patients with serum vitamin D sufficiency. A [dose](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44664&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-response meta-analysis of seven cohort studies with 7,808 participants also concluded that higher levels of 25(OH)D were associated with a reduction of mortality in patients with prostate cancer.[[32](#cit/section_9.32)] A study of 1,000 men who were followed for 23 years examined prediagnostic serum 25(OH)D and prostate cancer survival.[[33](#cit/section_9.33)] Men with higher serum 25(OH)D were less likely to die of prostate cancer (HR Q5 vs. Q1, 0.72; 95% CI, 0.52–0.99; *P*trend = .006).

One analysis examined 943 participants who were diagnosed with prostate cancer and enrolled in the Malmö Diet and Cancer Study. The relationship between prediagnostic levels of vitamin D (25(OH)D) and survival was examined.[[34](#cit/section_9.34)] The [mean](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285973&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) time from [diagnosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46450&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) until the end of [follow-up](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44671&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) was 9.1 years (standard deviation [SD], 4.5 years), and the mean time from inclusion until end of follow-up was 16.6 years (SD, 4.9 years). The study found a trend toward higher survival with vitamin D levels above 85 nmol/L. This finding became [statistically significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44167&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in the third quartile of 25(OH)D levels (85–102 nmol/L), compared with the first quartile (<68 nmol/L). The HR was 0.54 (0.34–0.85) when adjusted for age, time of inclusion, and [body mass index](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=455136&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The association was further strengthened when adjusted for age at diagnosis, [Gleason score](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45696&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and TNM (tumor, node, metastasis) classification, with an HR of 0.36 in the third quartile (0.22–0.60; *P* = .03).

One hundred ninety men who participated in a large epidemiological study underwent [radical prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46549&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for clinically [localized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45754&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer.[[35](#cit/section_9.35)] At the time of [prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45851&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 87 men (45.8%) exhibited adverse pathology, defined as primary Gleason 4, any Gleason 5, or extraprostatic extension. Men with adverse pathology had a lower [median](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44941&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) serum 25(OH)D (22.7 ng/mL) compared with their counterparts (27.0 ng/mL), and were also more likely to have a serum 25(OH)D level of less than 30 ng/mL.

In the MARTINI-Lifestyle cohort study, [biochemical recurrence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=543628&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (BCR) after radical prostatectomy was studied in 3,849 men who were followed for 3 years and had levels of serum 25(OH)D concentrations measured at the time of [surgery](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45570&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[36](#cit/section_9.36)] When stratified according to median vitamin D levels, the BCR-free survival rate at follow-up was 82.7% in patients with vitamin D levels of less than 19.3 μg/L and 83.0% in patients with levels of 19.3 μg/L or greater (*P* ≤ .59). The authors concluded that “a recommendation should therefore be made to compensate for a potential deficiency and not with the expectation of a reduction in the risk of progression."

In another retrospective study, 111 men with prostate cancer had serum levels of plasma 25(OH)D and 1,25(OH)2D measured at 4.9 years or 8.6 years postdiagnosis. An analysis examined all-cause and prostate-specific mortality.[[37](#cit/section_9.37)] Plasma 1,25(OH)2D levels (but not 25(OH)D levels) were inversely associated with all-cause mortality (HR for highest relative to lowest quartile,  0.45; 95% CI, 0.29–0.69) and prostate cancer-specific mortality (HR, 0.40; 95% CI, 0.14–1.19). In a subset analyses, these associations were apparent only in men with aggressive prostate cancer; the all-cause mortality HR was 0.28 (95% CI, 0.15–0.52; *P* = .07) and the prostate cancer-specific mortality HR was 0.26 (95% CI, 0.07–1.00).

A study of 943 patients with prostate cancer examined serum levels of vitamin D and aggressive prostate cancer.[[38](#cit/section_9.38)] There was a possible relationship between vitamin D and low-risk tumors. There were both positive and negative interactions between [parathyroid hormone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44339&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), calcium, and vitamin D and the risk of prostate cancer. These results were similar for low-risk and aggressive cases.

In a study of 155 African American men with prostate cancer, vitamin D levels were measured at diagnosis.[[39](#cit/section_9.39)] The study found that vitamin D deficiency (<20 ng/mL) significantly increased the risk of aggressive disease (OR, 3.1; 95% CI, 1.03–9.57; *P* = .04). Stratification by total calcium dietary intake showed that high calcium intake (≥800 mg/day) modified this association (OR, 7.3; 95% CI, 2.15–47.68; *P* interaction = .03). The genetic variant *rs11568820* appeared to increase the magnitude of association between deficient serum vitamin D levels and aggressive prostate cancer (OR, 3.64; 95% CI, 1.12–11.75; *P* = .05).

#### Interventional studies

In a 2009 study, patients with [locally advanced](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45955&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or metastatic prostate cancer and [asymptomatic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46520&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) progression of their PSA levels were treated with [vitamin D2](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655061&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (ergocalciferol) at either 10 [μg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=306521&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or 25 μg daily. The investigators reported that about 20% of these patients had at least a 25% drop in PSA level 3 months after initiating the vitamin D2.[[40](#cit/section_9.40)]

Calcitriol (1,25-dihydroxy vitamin D), the hormonally active form of vitamin D, has been the focus of some studies in prostate cancer patients. In an [open-label](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285990&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [phase II](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45831&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study, patients with recurrent prostate cancer were treated with calcitriol and [naproxen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=615394&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for 1 year. This treatment was effective in decreasing the rate of rising PSA levels in study participants, suggesting it may slow disease progression.[[41](#cit/section_9.41)] In a 2010 study, patients with castration-resistant prostate cancer were treated with calcitriol and [dexamethasone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45262&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The results indicated that while the treatments were well tolerated, they did not have an effect on participants' PSA levels.[[42](#cit/section_9.42)]

In a 2018 randomized controlled trial, men aged 50 years or older and women aged 55 years or older received vitamin D3 (cholecalciferol) and [omega-3 fatty acid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45809&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) supplements for the prevention of cancer and [cardiovascular disease](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=748137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The vitamin D supplement did not result in a lower incidence of any cancer, including prostate cancer, or cardiac disease compared with a [placebo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46688&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[43](#cit/section_9.43)]

A systematic review and meta-analysis of 16 before-after studies and 6 randomized controlled studies evaluated the effect of vitamin D [supplementation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45916&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) on PSA change, PSA response proportion, mortality, and [adverse effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44922&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The analysis of controlled clinical trials found no significant difference between vitamin D supplementation and the placebo groups for PSA change from baseline (weighted mean difference, -1.66 ng/mL; 95% CI, -0.69 to 0.36; *P* = .543), PSA response proportion (RP) (RP, 1.18; 95% CI, 0.97–1.45; *P* = .104), and mortality rate (RR, 1.05; 95% CI, 0.81–1.36; *P* = .713). Single-arm trials revealed that vitamin D supplementation had a modest effect on PSA response proportion. Nineteen percent of enrolled patients had at least a 50% reduction in PSA levels by the end of treatment (95% CI, 7%–31%; *P* = .002). The authors believed that the evidence from these studies did not show important benefits from vitamin D supplementation and thus such supplementation should not be recommended as part of treatment.[[44](#cit/section_9.44)]

A post hoc analysis was conducted on data from two randomized controlled trials. Patients with castration-resistant prostate cancer received the combination of a [statin](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390255&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and vitamin D with abiraterone (AA). The analysis reported that one study ([COU-AA-301](/clinicaltrials/NCT00638690)) found that the use of AA with a statin and vitamin D reduced the risk of death by 38% (*P* = .0007), while AA alone was associated with a decrease in the risk of death by 10% (*P* = .025). The second study ([COU-AA-302](/clinicaltrials/NCT00887198)) compared AA plus a statin and vitamin D with [prednisone](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45492&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) alone and found that the use of AA plus a statin and vitamin D was associated with a reduced risk of death by 26% (*P* = .0054).[[45](#cit/section_9.45)]

#### Symptom management

A small group of patients (N = 59) who underwent [androgen deprivation therapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=559086&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (ADT) were randomly assigned to receive high-dose vitamin D (600 [IU](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44764&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/day plus 50,000 IU/week), low-dose vitamin D (600 IU/day), or a placebo for 24 weeks.[[46](#cit/section_9.46)] Muscle mass was measured using bioelectric impedance analysis (BIA), and strength was measured before and after supplementation. Muscle mass improved with high-dose vitamin D3 supplementation, but strength did not. No other measures of cancer outcomes were reported.

### Adverse Effects

#### Vitamin D toxicity

In most cases, [symptoms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45022&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of vitamin D [toxicity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445093&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) are caused by [hypercalcemia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45363&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), but limited evidence suggests high concentrations of vitamin D may also be expressed in various organs, including the following:

- [Kidneys](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46325&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Bones.
- [Central nervous system](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46481&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- [Cardiovascular](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44005&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) system.

Symptoms of toxicity may be observed at an intake of 10,000 to 50,000 IU per day over a period of many years. Hypercalcemia results from the vitamin D–dependent increase in [intestinal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44046&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) absorption of calcium, leading to rapid increases in blood calcium levels. [Side effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46580&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) include loss of the [urinary](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44093&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) concentrating mechanism of the kidney tubule (resulting in polyuria and polydipsia), decrease in growth factor receptor, hypercalciuria, and the metastatic [calcification](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44317&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of soft tissues. The central [nervous system](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=446114&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) may also be affected, resulting in severe [depression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=430479&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [anorexia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44103&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[47](#cit/section_9.47)]

A systematic review of the interactions and [pharmacokinetics](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44324&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of vitamin D and drugs used for the treatment of cancer was published.[[48](#cit/section_9.48)] Based on the review, 26 articles met the inclusion criteria. Calcitriol was the most commonly administered form of vitamin D, and adults with prostate cancer and [solid tumors](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45301&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were the most well-represented populations in this systematic review. Hypercalcemia (at a dose of 74 μg/wk [3,000 IU]; 125 μg/wk [5,000 IU] with the addition of dexamethasone) was the most frequently reported side effect.

[Hypophosphatemia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=687890&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) was also observed in two studies [[49](#cit/section_9.49),[50](#cit/section_9.50)] that administered vitamin D in conjunction with docetaxel in men with prostate cancer. The authors concluded that no adverse effects were experienced beyond what was expected from high-dose calcitriol supplementation and was denoted as having a low risk of interaction. Some chemotherapeutic [regimens](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45864&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) appear to reduce serum 25(OH)-D3 and/or 1,25-D3.

A number of studies evaluated the safety and efficacy of high-dose calcitriol in conjunction with [chemotherapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45214&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) drugs in men with androgen-independent prostate cancer, [hormone-refractory](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686077&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer, and metastatic castration-resistant prostate cancer.[[50](#cit/section_9.50)-[52](#cit/section_9.52)] In the studies that used docetaxel plus calcitriol for men with androgen-independent prostate cancer, no increased toxicity was observed when compared with docetaxel alone.

In men with hormone-refractory prostate cancer, one study examined the activity and tolerability of weekly high-dose calcitriol (32 μg/wk [1,300 IU]) with docetaxel in patients who had previously received docetaxel treatment.[[49](#cit/section_9.49)] Calcitriol was given orally in three divided doses, and docetaxel was given intravenously (30 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/m2) with dexamethasone (8 mg) orally 12 hours before, at the time of, and 12 hours after docetaxel [administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=478733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Most of the side effects were expected toxicities related to the chemotherapy. Grade 2 hypercalcemia was observed in one patient. Administration of calcitriol was discontinued until hypercalcemia resolved. Supplementation was restarted after 2 weeks. In another patient, persistent grade 3 [fatigue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=321374&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) was observed, and treatment of calcitriol was discontinued as docetaxel was reduced.

#### Phase I trials

[Phase I](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) studies have looked at the [maximum tolerated dose](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=546597&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (MTD) of weekly intravenous and oral calcitriol in conjunction with various chemotherapy drugs for cancer treatment. One study examined the MTD of calcitriol in conjunction with [gefitinib](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=258356&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) at 250 mg/day ([oral chemotherapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=689103&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) used to treat [lung cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445043&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) in 32 patients with advanced solid tumors that were metastatic or [unresectable](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45936&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[53](#cit/section_9.53)] At doses up to 74 μg (3,000 IU) per week, no [dose-limiting](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44170&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) toxicities were observed. Grade 2 hypercalcemia was observed in two of four patients receiving 96 μg per week (3,900 IU) of calcitriol and was denoted nontolerable. No [significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) bone marrow suppression was observed at any dose. A dose of 74 μg (3,000 IU) per week was denoted as the MTD. The study suggests no major interaction between calcitriol and gefitinib.

A second phase I study examined the MTD and pharmacokinetics of calcitriol when administered with [paclitaxel](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45258&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) over the course of 6 weeks.[[54](#cit/section_9.54)] Thirty-six patients ([heterogenous](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44282&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) diagnoses) were enrolled in the trial and received escalating doses of oral calcitriol starting at 4 μg (160 IU) for 3 consecutive days, increasing to 38 μg (1,520 IU) with an 80-mg/m2 [infusion](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45738&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of paclitaxel given weekly. Results demonstrate that very high doses of calcitriol can be safely administered with paclitaxel. There was no dose-limiting toxicity in the trial, and at a dose of 38 μg/wk, no clinically significant hypercalcemia occurred. However, it is important to note that participants were administered from 8 to 76 [capsules](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=455334&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of calcitriol with no report of adherence to the [prescribed](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44711&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dose of calcitriol.

## Vitamin E

### Overview

- Most [dietary](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [vitamin E](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45023&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) comes from gamma-tocopherol. Food sources of vitamin E include vegetable oil, nuts, and egg yolks.
- Research suggests that vitamin E may protect against a number of [chronic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45641&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) diseases, such as [cardiovascular disease](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=748137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Studies suggest that [alpha-tocopherol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691423&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)–associated [protein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46092&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (TAP) may have capabilities as a [tumor suppressor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46657&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- The Selenium and Vitamin E Cancer Prevention Trial (SELECT), a large [multicenter](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44939&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [clinical trial](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45961&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), was initiated by the [National Institutes of Health](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44268&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (NIH) in 2001 to examine the effects of [selenium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45113&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and/or vitamin E on the development of prostate cancer.
- In 2011, updated results from SELECT showed that men who took vitamin E alone had a 17% increase in prostate cancer risk compared with men who took [placebo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46688&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- In 2014, an analysis of SELECT results showed that men who had high selenium status at [baseline](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and who were randomly assigned to receive selenium [supplementation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45916&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) had an increased risk of [high-grade](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=386205&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) prostate cancer, but vitamin E supplementation had no effect among men with high selenium status.

### General Information and History

Vitamin E was discovered in 1922 as a factor essential for reproduction.[[1](#cit/section_10.1)]

Vitamin E occurs in eight different forms: four tocopherols (alpha-, beta-, gamma-, and sigma-) and four tocotrienols (alpha-, beta-, gamma-, and sigma-).[[2](#cit/section_10.2)] Compared with other tocopherols, alpha-tocopherol (the form of vitamin E commonly found in dietary supplements) is the most abundant in the body and the most biologically active. Most dietary vitamin E comes from gamma-tocopherol. Food sources of vitamin E include vegetable oil, nuts, and egg yolks.[[3](#cit/section_10.3)]

The [bioavailability](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of vitamin E depends on a number of factors, such as the food matrix containing vitamin E (e.g., low- or high-fat food).[[4](#cit/section_10.4)] Vitamin E is delivered to [tissues](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46683&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) by high- and low-density lipoproteins (HDL and LDL, respectively). Delivery by LDL occurs via an endocytic pathway, while the protein’s [ATP](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44749&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-binding cassette, subfamily 1 and scavenger [receptor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44958&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) class B type 1 (SR-BI) are involved in HDL vitamin E transport.[[5](#cit/section_10.5)]

Research suggests that vitamin E may protect against a number of chronic diseases, such as cardiovascular disease.[[5](#cit/section_10.5)] Many of vitamin E’s health benefits have been ascribed to its actions as a powerful [antioxidant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43997&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov); as with other antioxidants, vitamin E protects [cell](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [membranes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46294&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) by interfering with reactions that would form [lipid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46068&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) hydroperoxide products.[[5](#cit/section_10.5)] Vitamin E also has nonantioxidant functions; it has been shown to [modulate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390300&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) signaling pathways and [gene expression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=537335&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[3](#cit/section_10.3)]

Companies distribute vitamin E as a dietary supplement. In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. The FDA can remove dietary supplements from the market that are deemed unsafe. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of vitamin E as a treatment for cancer.

### Human Studies

#### Epidemiological studies

The National Institutes of Health-American Association of Retired Persons (NIH-AARP) Diet and Health Study was initiated to examine whether supplemental vitamin E and dietary tocopherol intakes may prevent prostate cancer. Participants in the study completed food-frequency questionnaires and were [monitored](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454803&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for 5 years. No association between vitamin E supplements and prostate cancer risk was found. However, a reduction in the risk of advanced prostate cancer was observed with high intakes of gamma-tocopherol.[[6](#cit/section_10.6)]

In a 2010 study, levels of [trace elements](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44573&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and vitamin E were measured in prostate cancer patients who had significantly lower levels of [plasma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45839&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) vitamin E than did healthy controls. In addition, there was an inverse association between [prostate-specific antigen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46540&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels and plasma vitamin E.[[7](#cit/section_10.7)]

Studies suggest that alpha-tocopherol–associated protein (TAP) may have capabilities as a [tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) suppressor in prostate cancer. In a 2007 study, prostate cancer specimens, which had been obtained from [radical prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46549&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), were examined for TAP expression. Results showed reduced TAP expression in prostate cancer [tissue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46683&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and lower levels of TAP were associated with higher [clinical stage](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686244&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and larger tumor size.[[8](#cit/section_10.8)]

A study published in 2011 examined [serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) alpha-tocopherol and supplemental vitamin E intake with sex [steroid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=613764&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [hormones](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45713&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in participants in the Third National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey (NHANES III). Results showed an inverse association between serum alpha-tocopherol levels and sex steroid hormones, but only in smokers.[[9](#cit/section_10.9)]

Serum alpha-tocopherol and gamma-tocopherol levels and prostate cancer risk were examined in participants in the Prostate, Lung, Colorectal and Ovarian (PLCO) Screening Trial. An inverse relationship was observed between alpha-tocopherol levels and prostate cancer, but only in current and recently former smokers.[[10](#cit/section_10.10)] A [meta-analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=691484&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of nine nested [case-control studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=348989&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), representing approximately 370,000 men from several countries, also found an inverse relationship between [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) alpha-tocopherol levels and prostate cancer risk in all patients studied rather than limited to a smoking subset.[[11](#cit/section_10.11)] No association was seen with gamma-tocopherol levels in this analysis. The risk of prostate cancer decreased by 21% for every 25 mg/L increase in blood alpha-tocopherol levels.

The North Carolina-Louisiana Prostate Cancer Project investigated racial and geographic differences in prostate cancer [aggressiveness](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46053&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[12](#cit/section_10.12)] The effects of food intake of tocopherols, vitamin E supplementation, and adipose tissue [biomarkers](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45618&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of tocopherol were studied. In 1,023 African American men and 1,079 White men with incident prostate cancer, inverse associations were observed between dietary sources of tocopherol and prostate cancer aggressiveness that were [statistically significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44167&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in White men but not in African American men.

#### Interventional studies

The Physicians’ Health Study II investigated whether [vitamin C](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439435&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or vitamin E prevents prostate cancer and other [cancers](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in men. Participants were randomly assigned to receive vitamin E ([synthetic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=462950&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) alpha-tocopherol, 400 [IU](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44764&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) qod) and/or vitamin C (synthetic [ascorbic acid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439436&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 500 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/d) supplements and were monitored for an average of 8 years. The overall rates of prostate cancer were very similar in the vitamin E supplement and placebo groups, suggesting that vitamin E may not prevent prostate cancer. Furthermore, vitamin E did not have an effect on total cancer or [mortality](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=496502&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in these participants.[[13](#cit/section_10.13)]

Although not primarily designed for this purpose, the Alpha-Tocopherol, Beta-Carotene Cancer Prevention (ATBC) Study has been a resource for researchers investigating prostate cancer and vitamin E.[[14](#cit/section_10.14)] A long [follow-up](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44671&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study of participants in the ATBC Study was conducted. Baseline serum alpha-tocopherol levels and dietary intake of vitamin E was assessed and participants were monitored for up to 19 years. Findings revealed that while there was no association between dietary vitamin E levels and prostate cancer risk, higher serum alpha-tocopherol levels may be associated with a decreased risk for developing advanced prostate cancer.[[15](#cit/section_10.15)] In a 2009 study, blood samples obtained from participants in the ATBC Study were analyzed and genotyped. Results showed that [genetic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46391&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) variations in the *TTPA* and *SEC14L2* [genes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) were associated with serum alpha-tocopherol but did not directly affect prostate cancer risk. However, results suggested that [polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44805&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in *SEC14L2* may influence the effect of alpha-tocopherol supplementation on prostate cancer risk.[[16](#cit/section_10.16)] One study also focused on the ATBC Study and investigated whether serum alpha-tocopherol levels [affected](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=460124&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) survival time in men [diagnosed](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46450&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with prostate cancer. Serum alpha-tocopherol levels were assessed at baseline and 3 years later. Higher serum alpha-tocopherol levels, at both baseline and the 3-year point, were associated with improved prostate cancer survival.[[17](#cit/section_10.17)] Findings from 28 years of follow-up of the [cohort](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=561393&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) confirmed the lack of association between serum alpha-tocopherol levels and prostate cancer risk.[[18](#cit/section_10.18)] However, high alpha-tocopherol [concentration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=683342&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) was associated with a decreased risk of prostate cancer among participants in the trial who were supplemented with alpha-tocopherol (fifth quintile vs. first quintile; hazard ratio [HR], 0.79; 95% CI, 0.64–0.99).

A 2011 study examined links between serum alpha- and gamma-tocopherols and risk of prostate cancer among participants in the Carotene and Retinol Efficacy Trial (CARET). CARET was a [randomized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [placebo-controlled](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44840&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study that investigated whether daily supplementation of [beta-carotene](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45328&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [retinyl palmitate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46058&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) would reduce the risk of [lung cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445043&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in heavy smokers and [asbestos](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46528&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-exposed workers. Results indicated that among current smokers, higher levels of serum alpha- and gamma-tocopherols were associated with reduced risk of aggressive prostate cancer. In addition, findings suggested there may be an interaction between myeloperoxidase (MGO) G-463A [genotype](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=660739&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic), serum alpha-tocopherol level, and prostate cancer risk. There was an inverse relationship between prostate cancer risk and serum alpha-tocopherol levels in certain genotypes.[[19](#cit/section_10.19)]

##### The Selenium and Vitamin E Cancer Prevention Trial (SELECT)

On the basis of findings from earlier studies,[[14](#cit/section_10.14),[20](#cit/section_10.20)] the SELECT, a large [multicenter](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44939&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [clinical trial](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45961&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), was initiated by the NIH in 2001 to examine the effects of selenium and/or vitamin E on the development of prostate cancer. SELECT was a [phase III](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45833&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), randomized, [double-blind](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), placebo-controlled, population-based trial.[[21](#cit/section_10.21)] More than 35,000 men, aged 50 years or older, from more than 400 study sites in the United States, Canada, and Puerto Rico were randomly assigned to receive vitamin E (all-rac-alpha-tocopherol [acetate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655020&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 400 IU/d) and a placebo, selenium (L-selenomethionine, 200 µg/d) and a placebo, vitamin E and selenium, or two [placebos](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46688&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) daily for 7 to 12 years. The [primary endpoint](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44163&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the clinical trial was [incidence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46145&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer.[[21](#cit/section_10.21)]

Initial results of SELECT were published in 2009. There were no statistically significant differences in rates of prostate cancer in the four groups. In the vitamin E–alone group, there was a nonsignificant increase in rates of prostate cancer (*P* = .06); in the selenium–alone group, there was a nonsignificant increase in incidence of [diabetes mellitus](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44129&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (*P* = .16). On the basis of those findings, the data and safety monitoring committee recommended that participants stop taking the study supplements.[[22](#cit/section_10.22)]

Updated results were published in 2011. When compared with placebo, the rate of prostate cancer detection was significantly greater in the vitamin E–alone group (*P* = .008) and represented a 17% increase in prostate cancer risk. There was also greater incidence of prostate cancer in men who had taken selenium than in men who had taken placebo, but those differences were not statistically significant.[[23](#cit/section_10.23)]

Toenail selenium levels were [assayed](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44528&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in a two-case [cohort study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of a subset of SELECT participants. Vitamin E supplementation alone had no effect in men with high selenium status at baseline but increased the risks of total (63%; *P* = .02), [low-grade](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=386213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (46%; *P* = .09), and high-grade (111%; *P* = .008) prostate cancer among men with lower baseline selenium status. The authors concluded that men older than 55 years should avoid supplementation with either vitamin E or selenium at [doses](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44664&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) exceeding dietary recommendations.[[24](#cit/section_10.24)] In a case-cohort analysis of 1,434 men in the SELECT who underwent analysis of [single nucleotide polymorphisms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=458046&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in 21 genes, investigators found support for the [hypothesis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44209&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that [genetic variation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=776887&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in selenium and vitamin E metabolism/transport genes may influence the risk of overall- and high-grade prostate cancer and that selenium or vitamin E supplementation may modify an individual's [response](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44085&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) to those risks.[[25](#cit/section_10.25)]

The dose and form of vitamin E used in SELECT may have contributed to the results. On the basis of the results of the ATBC Study, all-rac-alpha-tocopheryl acetate was the form of vitamin E used in SELECT. The dose used in SELECT (400 IU) was higher than that in the ATBC Study. SELECT researchers opted for the higher dose because it was found in vitamin supplements, there was evidence for benefits of higher doses (including reductions in Alzheimer’s disease and [age-related macular degeneration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=642152&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)), and it was thought the higher dose would be more protective against prostate cancer than a lower dose.[[26](#cit/section_10.26)] A study of serum metabolomic response to vitamin E supplementation found that high-dose (400 IU/d) but not low-dose (50 IU/d) vitamin E resulted in a significant reduction in a novel C22 lactone sulfate that was highly correlated with alterations of androgenic steroid metabolites, possibly explaining the discordant results of the two trials.[[27](#cit/section_10.27)] Following the results of SELECT, it has been posited that high levels of alpha-tocopherol may affect levels of gamma-tocopherol, another form of vitamin E that may have [chemopreventive](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45487&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects.[[28](#cit/section_10.28)] Another important difference between the ATBC Study and SELECT that may explain the findings was the smoking status of study participants. Participants in the ATBC Study were smokers, while 7.5% of SELECT participants used [tobacco](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439432&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) products.[[29](#cit/section_10.29)]

#### Current Clinical Trials

Use our [advanced clinical trial search](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials-search/advanced") to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. [General information](https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials "https://www.cancer.gov/research/participate/clinical-trials") about clinical trials is also available.

### Adverse Effects

Alpha-tocopherols have been deemed Generally Recognized as Safe by the FDA.[[30](#cit/section_10.30)]

In the Physicians’ Health Study II, there were no significant adverse effects reported for [gastrointestinal tract](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46189&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [symptoms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45022&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [fatigue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=321374&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), drowsiness, skin discoloration or rashes, or migraine. However, participants who took vitamin E (alpha-tocopherol, 400 IU qod) experienced a greater number of hemorrhagic strokes than did participants who took placebo.[[13](#cit/section_10.13)] An increase in hemorrhagic strokes among participants in the vitamin E group (alpha-tocopherol, 50 mg/d) also was noted in the ATBC Study.[[14](#cit/section_10.14)]

In the initial report of results from SELECT, there were no significant differences between incidences of less severe [adverse effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44922&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (e.g., [alopecia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44642&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [dermatitis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45667&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and [nausea](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390302&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) experienced by the groups that received vitamin E (rac-alpha-tocopheryl acetate, 400 IU/d) and those experienced by the other treatment groups.[[22](#cit/section_10.22)] Follow-up analysis of SELECT participants revealed an increased risk of prostate cancer among men in the vitamin E–alone group.[[23](#cit/section_10.23)]
