## Multicomponent Therapies

### Pomi-T (Pomegranate, Green Tea, Broccoli, and Turmeric)

In a [placebo-controlled](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44840&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [double-blind](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [randomized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study, 199 men with [localized](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45754&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) were randomly assigned to either a food supplement, Pomi-T, or [placebo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46688&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (2:1) for 6 months.[[1](#cit/section_11.1)] Pomi-T contained 100 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) each of [pomegranate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=321386&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) whole fruit powder, broccoli powder, and [turmeric](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463050&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) powder; and 20 mg of [green tea extract](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=389271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (equivalent to 100 mg of tea). The [herbal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463714&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) ingredients in this supplement were raw, dry, powdered plant materials and one plant [extract](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407760&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), none of which were [chemically](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=643008&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) standardized. Chemical standardization is widely performed with herbal extracts, as a means of enhancing the reproducibility of studies with herbal [dietary supplements](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) via qualitative and quantitative chemical [analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390238&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). There were no [significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) differences in age or [Gleason score](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45696&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) between the groups. Forty percent of the patients had rising [prostate-specific antigen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46540&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (PSA) levels following [local therapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45755&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and 60% were on [active surveillance](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=616060&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (prelocal therapy). The study found a [median](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44941&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) rise in PSA of 14.7% after 6 months in the Pomi-T group compared with a 78.5% median rise in PSA in the placebo group. The supplement was well tolerated with no significant increase in [adverse events](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=444960&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) compared with placebo, although a trend was noted towards increased flatulence and loose [bowels](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46500&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in the supplement group.

Important differences exist between the various pomegranate preparations and their standardization. While dried fruit powder is commonly found in the marketplace, an equal amount of pomegranate fruit extract has a much higher content of [polyphenols](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=256573&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that are considered the bioactive constituents and can be used for the chemical standardization of preparations.

### Lycopene, Selenium, and Green Tea

In a randomized, [double-blinded](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45673&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), placebo-controlled study of a supplement containing [lycopene](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44909&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (35 mg), [selenium](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45113&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (55 µg), and green tea [catechins](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686469&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (600 mg) that was given for 6 months and targeted men with [high-grade](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=386205&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [prostatic intraepithelial neoplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44345&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (HGPIN) and/or atypical small acinar proliferation, a higher incidence of prostate cancer was seen on rebiopsy in men who received the supplement. Although the expected (or historical) rate of progression to prostate cancer is less than 20% (even at 1 year), more than 25.5% of this population of men had a [diagnosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46450&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of prostate cancer at 6 months, which may be attributed to inadequate sampling and potentially missed cancers at [baseline](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). A high percentage of positive biopsies raises the concern for cancers missed on baseline [biopsy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45164&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and further study is warranted.[[2](#cit/section_11.2)]

### Lycopene and Other Components

One study randomly assigned 79 men before [prostatectomy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45851&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) to a [nutritional](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44698&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [intervention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454757&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) with tomato products containing 30 mg of lycopene daily; tomato products plus selenium, [omega-3 fatty acids](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45809&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [soy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407766&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [isoflavones](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), grape/pomegranate juice, and green/black tea; or a control [diet](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) for 3 weeks.[[3](#cit/section_11.3)] There were no differences in PSA values between the intervention and [control groups](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44149&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). However, a post hoc exploratory analysis found lower PSA values in men with intermediate-risk prostate cancer who consumed the tomato products and in men with the highest increases in lycopene levels.

### Zyflamend

#### Overview

- Zyflamend is a dietary supplement that contains supercritical [fluid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44669&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (CO2) and hydroalcoholic extracts of the following herbs, combined and suspended in olive oil: rosemary, turmeric, [ginger](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=340930&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), holy basil, green tea, hu zhang, Chinese goldthread, barberry, oregano, and Baikal skullcap.
- The individual components of Zyflamend have [anti-inflammatory](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44187&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and possible [anticarcinogenic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44272&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) properties.
- In various [preclinical studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44517&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), Zyflamend has been shown to suppress the expression of certain [genes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) involved in the [inflammatory](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373080&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [response](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44085&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and in [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [progression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44078&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), such as cyclooxygenase 1 (COX-1), [COX-2](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=589403&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), 5-lipoxygenase (5-LOX), and 12-LOX.
- In other preclinical studies, Zyflamend demonstrated single-agent anticancer activity, and it improved cancer suppression when used with hormonal and [chemotherapy](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45214&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) agents.
- Results of a [phase I](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45830&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) study of Zyflamend suggest that use of this supplement is not associated with serious [toxicity](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445093&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or [adverse effects](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44922&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

#### General information and history

Zyflamend is a dietary supplement that contains CO2 and hydroalcoholic extracts of the following herbs, combined and suspended in olive oil:

- Rosemary (*Rosmarinus officinalis* L.).
- Turmeric (*Curcuma longa* L.).
- Ginger (*Zingiber officinale* Roscoe).
- Holy basil (*Ocimum sanctum* L.).
- Green tea (*Camellia sinensis* [L.] Kuntze).
- Hu zhang (*Polygonum cuspidatum* Siebold & Zucc.).
- Chinese goldthread (*Coptis chinensis* Franch.).
- Barberry (*Berberis vulgaris* L.).
- Oregano (*Origanum vulgare* L.).
- Baikal skullcap (*Scutellaria baicalensis* Georgi).

The individual components of Zyflamend have anti-inflammatory and possible anticarcinogenic properties. For example, results of a 2011 study suggest that Zyflamend may inhibit the growth of [melanoma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45135&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[4](#cit/section_11.4)]

The extracts in Zyflamend have been shown to have anti-inflammatory effects via inhibition of cyclooxygenase (COX) activity. COXs are [enzymes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46081&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that convert arachidonic [acid](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=642987&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) into [prostaglandins](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=559143&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), which are thought to play a role in [tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) development and [metastasis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46710&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). One COX enzyme, COX-2, is activated during [chronic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45641&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) disease states, such as cancer.[[5](#cit/section_11.5)]

The antitumorigenic mechanisms of action of Zyflamend are unknown, but according to one study, Zyflamend may suppress activation of [nuclear factor-kappa B](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=773861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (NF-kappa B) (a nuclear [transcription](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390290&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) factor involved in tumorigenesis) and NF-kappa B–regulated gene products.[[6](#cit/section_11.6)]

Several companies distribute Zyflamend as a dietary supplement. In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of Zyflamend as a treatment for cancer or any other medical [condition](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651193&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

#### Preclinical/animal studies

##### *In vitro* studies

In a study reported in 2012, human [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) cells were treated *[in vitro](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* with Zyflamend. Cells treated with the supplement at concentrations ranging from 0.06 to 0.5 [μL](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655121&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/[mL](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=655103&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) exhibited [dose-dependent](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44110&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) decreases in [androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [receptor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44958&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and PSA expression levels compared with cells treated with the [dimethyl sulfoxide](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44097&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) vehicle control. Prostate cancer cells that were treated with a combination of Zyflamend (0.06 μL/mL) and [bicalutamide](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45354&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (25 [μM](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=659790&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)), an androgen receptor inhibitor, showed reductions in cell growth, PSA expression, and [antiapoptotic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390240&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [protein expression](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=557359&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) compared with cells treated with Zyflamend or bicalutamide alone.[[7](#cit/section_11.7)]

Although the individual components of Zyflamend have been shown to influence COX activity, one study examined the effects of the drug on COX-1 and COX-2 expression in prostate cancer cells. The results revealed that Zyflamend, at a [concentration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=683342&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of 0.9 μL/mL, inhibited expression of both COX-1 and COX-2. At a concentration of 0.45 μL/mL, the degree of COX-2 inhibition was observed, but the level of COX-1 inhibition was reduced by 50%. At a concentration of 0.1 μL/mL, Zyflamend effectively inhibited growth of prostate cancer cells and increased the level of caspase-3, a proapoptotic enzyme. However, a separate experiment indicated that the prostate cancer cells used in the study (LNCaP cells, which are androgen sensitive) did not express high levels of COX-2, suggesting that Zyflamend’s effects on prostate cancer cells may result from a COX-independent mechanism.[[5](#cit/section_11.5)]

The lipoxygenase isozymes 5-LOX and 12-LOX are also proteins associated with [inflammation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and tumor growth. In a 2007 study, the effects of Zyflamend on 5-LOX and 12-LOX expression were investigated. The findings indicated that 0.25 μL/mL to 2 μL/mL of Zyflamend produced decreases in 5-LOX and 12-LOX expression in PC3 prostate cancer cells (cells that have high [metastatic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44058&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) potential). The supplement also inhibited [cell proliferation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46479&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and induced [apoptosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46524&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). In addition, Zyflamend treatment resulted in a decrease in Rb [phosphorylation](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=641137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (Rb proteins control [cell-cycle](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=597111&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-related genes). These results indicate that Zyflamend may inhibit prostate cancer cell growth through a variety of mechanisms.[[8](#cit/section_11.8)]

In a 2011 study, human prostate cancer cells were treated with Zyflamend (200 [µg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=306521&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/mL). After 48 hours of treatment, a [statistically significant](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44167&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [reduction](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=654999&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in cell growth was observed for Zyflamend-treated cells, compared with control cells (*P* < .005). In another experiment, prostate cancer cells were treated with [insulin-like growth factor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=653119&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-1 (IGF-1; 0–100 [ng](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285977&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/mL) alone or in combination with Zyflamend (200 µg/mL). Cells treated with IGF-1 alone exhibited statistically significant, dose-dependent increases in cell proliferation, whereas cells treated with both IGF-1 and Zyflamend showed significant decreases in cell proliferation. Zyflamend was also shown to decrease cellular levels of the IGF-1 receptor and the androgen receptor in prostate cancer cells.[[9](#cit/section_11.9)] A 2014 investigation by this team found that Zyflamend inhibits the expression of class I and class II [histone deacetylases](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=318815&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (HDAC) and upregulated their downstream target p21 suppressor gene.[[10](#cit/section_11.10)] The extracts of the individual components of the 10 botanicals in Zyflamend were also evaluated in an effort to identify which compounds contributed most to the inhibition of HDAC expression. Chinese goldthread and baikal skullcap appeared to be the most likely major contributors to the overall Zyflamend effect on HDAC expression.

##### Animal studies

Additional evidence that Zyflamend promotes apoptosis in cancer cells was obtained in laboratory and [animal studies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454774&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) reported in 2012.[[11](#cit/section_11.11)] Treatment of human [colorectal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46685&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [carcinoma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45963&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [cell lines](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44016&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) *in vitro* with Zyflamend was shown to significantly down regulate expression of antiapoptotic proteins, up regulate expression of Bax (a proapoptotic [protein](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46092&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)), and increase expression of [death receptor 5](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=641151&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (DR5), a receptor important in apoptosis. Moreover, when nude mice with [pancreatic cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44521&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) cell implants were randomly assigned to receive Zyflamend or a control treatment for 4 weeks, tumor cells from the Zyflamend-treated mice showed significant reductions in antiapoptotic proteins and significantly increased expression of DR5, compared with tumor cells from control-treated animals.

In a 2011 study, mice were also implanted with pancreatic cancer cells and then treated with [gemcitabine](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=508951&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and/or Zyflamend. The combination treatment resulted in a significantly greater decrease in tumor growth than did treatment with gemcitabine or Zyflamend alone. Other findings from this study suggest that Zyflamend exerted its effects by sensitizing the pancreatic tumors to gemcitabine through suppression of multiple targets linked to tumorigenesis.[[12](#cit/section_11.12)]

#### Human studies

##### Interventional studies

In one [case report](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44007&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), a patient with HGPIN received Zyflamend 3 times daily for 18 months. Zyflamend did not affect this patient's PSA level, but, after 18 months, repeat core [biopsies](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45164&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of the prostate did not show PIN or cancer.[[13](#cit/section_11.13)]

In a 2009 phase I study designed to assess safety and toxicity, patients with HGPIN were assigned to take Zyflamend (780 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) 3 times daily for 18 months, plus combinations of dietary supplements (i.e., [probiotic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=658861&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) supplement, multivitamin, green and white tea extract, Baikal skullcap, docosahexaenoic acid, holy basil, and turmeric). Zyflamend and the additional dietary supplements were well tolerated by the patients, and no serious adverse events occurred. After 18 months of treatment, 60% of the study subjects had only [benign](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45614&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [tissue](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46683&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) at biopsy; 26.7% had HGPIN in one core; and 13.3% had prostate cancer.[[14](#cit/section_11.14)]

#### Adverse effects

Zyflamend was well tolerated in the previously described 2009 [clinical study](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44195&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Mild heartburn was reported in 9 of 23 subjects, but it resolved when the study supplements were taken with food. No serious toxicity or adverse events were reported in the study.[[14](#cit/section_11.14)]

## Other Prostate Health Supplements

### Overview

Many widely available [dietary supplements](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=373932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) are marketed to support [prostate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46539&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) health. African cherry (*Pygeum africanum*) and [beta-sitosterol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=773858&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) are two related supplements that have been studied as potential [prostate cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=445079&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) treatments. Note: A separate [PDQ](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44271&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) summary on [PC-SPES](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/pc-spes-pdq) is also available.

Several companies distribute medicinal *P. africanum* or beta-sitosterol as dietary supplements. In the United States, dietary supplements are regulated by the [U.S. Food and Drug Administration](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454785&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (FDA) as a separate category from foods, cosmetics, and drugs. Unlike drugs, dietary supplements do not require premarket evaluation and approval by the FDA unless specific disease [prevention](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=439419&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or treatment claims are made. The quality and amount of ingredients in dietary supplements are also regulated by the FDA through Good Manufacturing Practices (GMPs). The FDA GMPs requires that every finished batch of dietary supplement meets each product specification for identity, purity, strength, composition, and limits on contamination that may [adulterate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=686042&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) dietary supplements. Because dietary supplements are not formally reviewed for manufacturing consistency every year, ingredients may vary considerably from lot to lot and there is no guarantee that ingredients claimed on product labels are present (or are present in the specified amounts). The FDA has not approved the use of *P. africanum* or beta-sitosterol as a treatment for [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or any other medical [condition](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=651193&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

### African Cherry/*Pygeum africanum*

*P. africanum* is a tree from the Rosaceae family that grows in tropical zones. It is found in a number of African countries including Kenya, Madagascar, Uganda, and Nigeria. Bark from the *P. africanum* tree was used by African tribes to treat [urinary](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44093&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [symptoms](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45022&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and [gastric](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46770&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) pain.[[1](#cit/section_12.1)] In the 18th century, European travelers learned from South African tribes that *P. africanum* was used to treat [bladder](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46501&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) discomfort and *old man’s disease* (enlarged prostate).

Since 1969, bark [extracts](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407760&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) from *P. africanum* have been available as [prescription](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44711&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) drugs in Europe and have been widely used to treat [benign prostatic hyperplasia](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46509&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[2](#cit/section_12.2),[3](#cit/section_12.3)] The bark contains a number of [compounds](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=422394&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) including [saturated](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463732&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and unsaturated [fatty acids](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45689&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), [phytosterols](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44485&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (e.g., beta-sitosterol), pentacyclic triterpenoids (e.g., oleanolic acid), alcohols, and [carbohydrates](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285960&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). The extract is obtained by macerating and solubilizing the bark in an organic [solvent](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463162&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), and evaporation of the solvent.[[1](#cit/section_12.1)]

Two components of *P. africanum* bark extracts, atraric acid and N-butylbenzene-[sulfonamide](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=367474&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), are [androgen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45592&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [receptor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44958&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) inhibitors, as indicated by both *[in vitro](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45733&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* [[4](#cit/section_12.4)-[6](#cit/section_12.6)] and [animal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=454774&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) *[in vivo](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46352&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)* [[7](#cit/section_12.7)] studies. This activity is produced by each of these components at [concentrations](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=683342&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) that are significantly lower than the clinically achieved concentration of the [antiandrogen](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46052&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [flutamide](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45264&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[8](#cit/section_12.8)]

### Beta-Sitosterol

Beta-sitosterol is a member of the phytosterol family of [phytochemicals](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44709&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). It is found ubiquitously in plants and has recently been classified as an invalid/improbable metabolic [panacea](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44073&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (IMP) compound.[[9](#cit/section_12.9)]  *Pygeum ®um*, [saw palmetto](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=514413&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (*Serenoa repens*), and some legumes can contain rather high concentrations. As a type of phytosterol (or [plant sterol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44161&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)), beta-sitosterol has a similar structure to [cholesterol](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=407756&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov). Phytosterols, including beta-sitosterol, reduce [absorption](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=463702&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) of dietary cholesterol and their potential to protect against [cardiovascular disease](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=748137&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) is under investigation. [Mean](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=285973&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [plasma](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45839&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) beta-sitosterol concentration in a small group of healthy male volunteers in Vienna, Austria, was 2.83 [μg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=306521&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/[mL](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44214&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) (approximately 7 [μM](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=372932&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)).[[10](#cit/section_12.10)] Interestingly, however, a rare condition caused by [mutations](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46063&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) in the [adenosine triphosphate](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45009&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)-binding cassette (ABC) transporter *ABCG5* or *ABCG8* [genes](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45693&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=genetic) results in an [inherited](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45098&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) sterol storage disease with markedly increased [serum](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44088&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) concentrations of plant sterols such as sitosterol and leads to premature atherosclerosis and large xanthomas.[[11](#cit/section_12.11)]

Research has also suggested that phytosterols may have [anticarcinogenic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44272&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) properties, but the exact mechanisms are unknown.[[12](#cit/section_12.12)] Phytosterols may exert [antitumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=446109&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) effects by acting on immune and hormonal systems, or by directly targeting [cell cycles](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=597111&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) and inducing [apoptosis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46524&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) in [tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [cells](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46476&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).[[13](#cit/section_12.13)]

Beta-sitosterol at very high concentrations (i.e., 16 μM or 6.64 [mg](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44213&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)/mL) has been shown to significantly inhibit growth of PC-3 prostate cancer cells and induce apoptosis.[[14](#cit/section_12.14),[15](#cit/section_12.15)] Beta-sitosterol is very poorly [bioavailable](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44225&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), with an estimated 0.41% of dietary beta-sitosterol absorbed, and circulating [blood](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270735&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) levels of about 3 μg/mL to 9 μg/mL in individuals consuming [diets](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44660&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) containing normal to high amounts of plant-based foods (approximately 1,000 times less than the concentration used in the study).[[10](#cit/section_12.10),[16](#cit/section_12.16)] Associated with these effects are decreasing levels of cell cycle regulators p21 and p27 in the cancer cells and an increased production of [reactive oxygen species](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=687227&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).

## Summary of the Evidence for Prostate Cancer, Nutrition, and Dietary Supplements

To assist readers in evaluating the results of human studies of integrative, alternative, and complementary therapies for [cancer](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45333&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), the strength of the evidence (i.e., the [levels of
evidence](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=446533&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) associated with each type of treatment is provided whenever
possible. To qualify for a level of evidence [analysis](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=390238&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), a study must:

- Be published in a [peer-reviewed scientific journal](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=537399&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Report on a [therapeutic](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=43985&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [outcome](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=467853&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) or outcomes, such as [tumor](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=46634&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) [response](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44085&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov), improvement in survival, or measured improvement in [quality of life](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=45417&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov).
- Describe [clinical](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44168&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) findings in sufficient detail that a meaningful evaluation can be made.

Separate levels of evidence scores are assigned to qualifying human studies on the basis of statistical strength of the study design and [scientific](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44724&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov) strength of the treatment outcomes (i.e., [endpoints](/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=346519&version=healthprofessional&language=English&dictionary=Cancer.gov)) measured. The resulting two scores are then combined to produce an overall score. For an explanation of the scores and additional information about levels of evidence analysis and scores, see [Levels of Evidence for Human Studies of Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies](/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/cam).

## Latest Updates to This Summary (12/20/2024)

The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as
new information becomes available. This section describes the latest
changes made to this summary as of the date above.

**[Introduction](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_47)**

Revised [text](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_49) to state that on the basis of data from 2018 to 2021, it is estimated that 12.8% of U.S. men will be diagnosed with prostate cancer during their lifetimes.

This summary is written and maintained by the [PDQ Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies Editorial Board](https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/cam "https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/cam"), which is
editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of
the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More
information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in
maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the [About This PDQ Summary](/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq#_AboutThis_1) and [PDQ® Cancer Information for Health Professionals](https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq "https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq") pages.

## About This PDQ Summary

### Purpose of This Summary

This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the use of nutrition and dietary supplements for reducing the risk of developing prostate cancer or for treating prostate cancer. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.

### Reviewers and Updates

This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the [PDQ Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies Editorial Board](https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/cam "https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/cam"), which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).

Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:

- be discussed at a meeting,
- be cited with text, or
- replace or update an existing article that is already cited.

Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.

Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's [Email Us](https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us "https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us"). Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.

### Levels of Evidence

Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies Editorial Board uses a [formal evidence ranking system](/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/cam) in developing its level-of-evidence designations.

### Permission to Use This Summary

PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as “NCI’s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].”

The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:

PDQ® Integrative, Alternative, and Complementary Therapies Editorial Board. PDQ Prostate Cancer, Nutrition, and Dietary Supplements. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated <MM/DD/YYYY>. Available at: [https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq](https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq "https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/cam/hp/prostate-supplements-pdq"). Accessed <MM/DD/YYYY>. [PMID: 26389500]

Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in [Visuals Online](https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/ "https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/"), a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

The information in these summaries should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the [Managing Cancer Care](https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care "https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care") page.

Questions can also be submitted to Cancer.gov through the website’s [Email Us](https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us "https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us").
